Table 1
Physics Formula Bank
1) Core mathematical tools
Average = (x1 + x2 + ... + xn) / n
Weighted average = Σ(wi xi) / Σ(wi)
Percent change = (new - old) / old × 100%
Rate = change / time
Slope = (y2 - y1) / (x2 - x1)
Distance = √((x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2)
Quadratic formula: x = (-b ± √(b^2 - 4ac)) / (2a)
Sum of series = Σxi
Product rule idea: if y = uv, then dy/dx = u(dv/dx) + v(du/dx)
2) SI units and prefixes
Length: m
Mass: kg
Time: s
Current: A
Temperature: K
Amount: mol
Luminous intensity: cd
Force: N = kg·m/s^2
Energy: J = N·m = kg·m^2/s^2
Power: W = J/s = kg·m^2/s^3
Pressure: Pa = N/m^2 = kg/(m·s^2)
Charge: C = A·s
Potential difference: V = J/C
Resistance: Ω = V/A
Frequency: Hz = 1/s
Prefixes: k = 10^3, M = 10^6, G = 10^9, m = 10^-3, μ = 10^-6, n = 10^-9
3) Dimensional analysis
[velocity] = L/T
[acceleration] = L/T^2
[force] = MLT^-2
[energy] = ML^2T^-2
[power] = ML^2T^-3
[pressure] = ML^-1T^-2
[momentum] = MLT^-1
[charge] = IT
[electric field] = MLT^-3I^-1
[resistance] = ML^2T^-3I^-2
Dimensional homogeneity: dimensions on both sides of an equation must match
4) Scalars and vectors
|A| = √(Ax^2 + Ay^2 + Az^2)
Component form: A = Ax i + Ay j + Az k
Addition: Rx = Ax + Bx, Ry = Ay + By, Rz = Az + Bz
Subtraction: A - B = (Ax - Bx, Ay - By, Az - Bz)
Unit vector: Â = A / |A|
Dot product: A · B = |A||B| cos θ = AxBx + AyBy + AzBz
Angle between vectors: cos θ = (A · B) / (|A||B|)
Cross product: |A × B| = |A||B| sin θ
5) Trigonometric essentials
sin θ = opposite / hypotenuse
cos θ = adjacent / hypotenuse
tan θ = opposite / adjacent
tan θ = sin θ / cos θ
sin^2 θ + cos^2 θ = 1
1 + tan^2 θ = sec^2 θ
1 + cot^2 θ = csc^2 θ
sin(-θ) = -sin θ
cos(-θ) = cos θ
tan(-θ) = -tan θ
6) Unit circle and angle conversion
θ(rad) = θ(deg) × π/180
θ(deg) = θ(rad) × 180/π
Arc length s = rθ
Sector area A = 1/2 r^2 θ
x = r cos θ
y = r sin θ
7) Kinematics in one dimension
v = dx/dt
a = dv/dt = d^2x/dt^2
v_avg = Δx/Δt
a_avg = Δv/Δt
v = v0 + at
x = x0 + v0 t + 1/2 at^2
v^2 = v0^2 + 2a(x - x0)
x - x0 = (v0 + v)t/2
For free fall: a = -g or +g depending on sign convention
8) Free fall and vertical motion
y = y0 + v0y t - 1/2 g t^2
vy = v0y - gt
vy^2 = v0y^2 - 2g(y - y0)
Time to top: t_up = v0y/g
Maximum height: Δy_max = v0y^2 / (2g)
Symmetry in same launch/landing level: t_total = 2v0y/g
9) Projectile motion
x = x0 + v0 cos θ · t
y = y0 + v0 sin θ · t - 1/2 g t^2
vx = v0 cos θ
vy = v0 sin θ - gt
Range (same height): R = v0^2 sin(2θ) / g
Time of flight (same height): T = 2v0 sin θ / g
Maximum height: H = v0^2 sin^2 θ / (2g)
10) Relative motion
vA/B = vA - vB
xA/B = xA - xB
Boat/rain/river problems use vector addition of velocity
Ground velocity = velocity relative to medium + medium velocity
11) Newton’s laws
ΣF = ma
Inertia: if ΣF = 0, velocity is constant
Action-reaction: FAB = -FBA
Weight: W = mg
Normal force often balances perpendicular components
Net force in components: ΣFx = max, ΣFy = may, ΣFz = maz
12) Common forces
Weight: W = mg
Spring force: Fs = -kx
Friction: fs ≤ μs N
Kinetic friction: fk = μk N
Tension often acts along a rope
Drag often modeled as Fd = bv or Fd = cv^2
13) Inclined plane
Parallel weight component: mg sin θ
Perpendicular weight component: mg cos θ
Normal force: N = mg cos θ (if no other vertical forces)
Acceleration down slope without friction: a = g sin θ
With friction: a = g(sin θ - μk cos θ) if sliding downhill
14) Circular motion
v = ωr
a_c = v^2/r = ω^2 r
F_c = mv^2/r = mω^2 r
ω = dθ/dt
α = dω/dt
v_t = rω
a_t = rα
Total acceleration: a = √(a_t^2 + a_c^2)
15) Rotation basics
θ = θ0 + ω0 t + 1/2 αt^2
ω = ω0 + αt
ω^2 = ω0^2 + 2α(θ - θ0)
θ - θ0 = (ω0 + ω)t/2
τ = rF sin θ
τ = Iα
L = Iω
For a particle: L = r × p
16) Moment of inertia
Point mass: I = mr^2
Rod about center: I = 1/12 ML^2
Rod about end: I = 1/3 ML^2
Solid disk/cylinder: I = 1/2 MR^2
Thin hoop: I = MR^2
Solid sphere: I = 2/5 MR^2
Thin spherical shell: I = 2/3 MR^2
Parallel-axis theorem: I = Icm + Md^2
17) Rotational energy and rolling
Krot = 1/2 Iω^2
Ktotal = 1/2 mv^2 + 1/2 Iω^2
Rolling without slipping: v = ωR
a = αR
Energy conservation with rotation: initial potential = translational + rotational + final potential
18) Work and energy
W = Fd cos θ
W = ∫ F · ds
Power: P = W/t
Instantaneous power: P = F · v
K = 1/2 mv^2
Wnet = ΔK
U_g = mgh near Earth
U_s = 1/2 kx^2
Mechanical energy: E = K + U
19) Conservative forces and potential
F = -dU/dx in one dimension
F = -∇U in vector form
ΔU = -Wcons
Energy conservation: K_i + U_i = K_f + U_f if no nonconservative work
With nonconservative work: Wnc = Δ(K + U)
20) Momentum and impulse
p = mv
J = FΔt = Δp
Σp_i = Σp_f if isolated system
Center of mass: r_cm = Σm_i r_i / Σm_i
v_cm = Σm_i v_i / Σm_i
Fext = M a_cm
21) Collisions
Perfectly inelastic: objects stick together
Elastic collision: momentum and kinetic energy conserved
Coefficient of restitution: e = relative speed after / relative speed before
1D momentum: m1v1i + m2v2i = m1v1f + m2v2f
Perfectly inelastic final speed: vf = (m1v1 + m2v2)/(m1 + m2)
22) Gravitation
F = Gm1m2/r^2
g = GM/r^2
U = -GMm/r
V_g = -GM/r
v_orbit = √(GM/r)
T_orbit = 2π√(r^3/GM)
Escape speed: v_esc = √(2GM/r)
Kepler’s third law: T^2 ∝ r^3
23) Fluids
Density: ρ = m/V
Pressure: P = F/A
Hydrostatic pressure: P = P0 + ρgh
Pascal’s principle: ΔP transmitted equally in enclosed fluid
Buoyant force: FB = ρfluid g Vdisplaced
Continuity: A1v1 = A2v2
Bernoulli: P + 1/2 ρv^2 + ρgh = constant
Flow rate: Q = Av
24) Surface tension and capillarity
Surface tension force: F = γL
Excess pressure in bubble: ΔP = 2γ/r for soap bubble, 4γ/r for double surface context as appropriate
Capillary rise: h = 2γ cos θ / (ρgr)
25) Temperature and thermal expansion
K = °C + 273.15
ΔL = αL0ΔT
ΔA = βA0ΔT
ΔV = γV0ΔT
For isotropic solids: β ≈ 2α, γ ≈ 3α
26) Heat and calorimetry
Q = mcΔT
Q = mL
Heat balance: ΣQ = 0 in isolated mixing problems
Specific heat: c = Q/(mΔT)
Latent heat: L = Q/m
27) Heat transfer
Conduction: Q/t = kAΔT/L
Convection often modeled qualitatively or by Q/t = hAΔT
Radiation: P = εσA(T^4 - Tenv^4)
Stefan-Boltzmann constant: σ
28) Ideal gas law
PV = nRT
PV = NkBT
R = 8.314 J/(mol·K)
Average translational kinetic energy: Kavg = 3/2 kBT
Pressure from molecular motion: P = 1/3 (N/V)mvrms^2
29) Gas processes
Isothermal: PV = constant
Isobaric: V/T = constant
Isochoric: P/T = constant
Adiabatic: PV^γ = constant
Work by gas: W = ∫ P dV
For constant pressure: W = PΔV
30) Thermodynamics fundamentals
First law: ΔU = Q - W
W is work done by the system in many conventions
Heat engine efficiency: e = Wout/Qin = 1 - Qout/Qin
Carnot efficiency: e = 1 - Tc/Th
Coefficient of performance (refrigerator): COP = Qc/W
COP (heat pump): COP = Qh/W
31) Entropy and second law
ΔS = Qrev/T
For isolated system: ΔS ≥ 0
Entropy units: J/K
Reversible process gives equality in idealized case
32) Oscillations: SHM
x(t) = A cos(ωt + φ) or A sin(ωt + φ)
v = dx/dt = -Aω sin(ωt + φ)
a = d^2x/dt^2 = -ω^2 x
ω = 2πf = 2π/T
T = 1/f
Maximum speed: vmax = Aω
Maximum acceleration: amax = Aω^2
33) Mass-spring oscillator
ω = √(k/m)
T = 2π√(m/k)
E = 1/2 kA^2 = 1/2 mω^2A^2
Energy exchange: K + U = constant in ideal SHM
34) Simple pendulum
For small angles: T = 2π√(L/g)
ω = √(g/L)
Small-angle approximation: sin θ ≈ θ
35) Waves: general relations
v = fλ
ω = 2πf
k = 2π/λ
y(x,t) = A sin(kx - ωt + φ) or A cos(kx - ωt + φ)
Wave speed on a string: v = √(T/μ)
μ = m/L
36) Superposition and interference
Resultant displacement = sum of individual displacements
Constructive interference: path difference = mλ
Destructive interference: path difference = (m + 1/2)λ
Beat frequency: fbeat = |f1 - f2|
37) Standing waves
For string fixed at both ends: λn = 2L/n
fn = nv/(2L), n = 1,2,3,...
For open pipe: same as string fixed at both ends
For closed pipe: λn = 4L/n for odd n only
fn = nv/(4L), n = 1,3,5,...
38) Sound
v = fλ
Intensity: I = P/A
Sound level: β = 10 log10(I/I0)
I0 = 10^-12 W/m^2
Frequency heard changes with Doppler effect
Doppler (general idea): observed frequency changes when source or observer moves
39) Doppler effect
For sound in still air, one common form: f' = f (v ± vo) / (v ∓ vs)
Use sign choice based on whether observer/source move toward or away from each other
v = speed of sound in medium
40) Reflection and mirrors
Angle of incidence = angle of reflection
Mirror equation: 1/f = 1/do + 1/di
Magnification: m = hi/ho = -di/do
Concave mirror usually f > 0 under standard sign convention
Convex mirror usually f < 0 under standard sign convention
41) Refraction
n = c/v
Snell’s law: n1 sin θ1 = n2 sin θ2
Critical angle: sin θc = n2/n1 for n1 > n2
Total internal reflection occurs when θ1 > θc
42) Lenses
Thin lens equation: 1/f = 1/do + 1/di
Magnification: m = hi/ho = -di/do
Lens power: P = 1/f (f in meters, P in diopters)
Converging lens: f > 0
Diverging lens: f < 0
43) Lens and mirror sign convention reminders
Object distance do usually positive for real objects
Image distance di sign depends on image side and convention
Focal length sign depends on converging/diverging or concave/convex element
Always apply one sign convention consistently
44) Wave optics
Double-slit bright fringe: d sin θ = mλ
Double-slit dark fringe: d sin θ = (m + 1/2)λ
Small-angle screen position: y ≈ mλL/d
Single-slit minima: a sin θ = mλ, m = 1,2,3,...
Diffraction grating: d sin θ = mλ
45) Polarization
Malus’s law: I = I0 cos^2 θ
Polarized light intensity after analyzer depends on angle between transmission axes
46) Electrostatics: charge and force
q = ne
e = 1.602 × 10^-19 C
Coulomb’s law: F = k q1q2 / r^2
k = 1/(4πε0)
Electric force is vector along line joining charges
Superposition applies to forces and fields
47) Electric field
E = F/q
Field of point charge: E = kq/r^2
Field direction is away from positive charge and toward negative charge
Field lines never cross
Field inside a conductor in electrostatic equilibrium = 0
48) Electric potential and potential energy
V = U/q
Potential of point charge: V = kq/r
ΔV = -∫ E · ds
Uniform field: ΔV = -Ed if along field direction
U = qV
ΔU = qΔV
49) Gauss’s law
∮ E · dA = qenc/ε0
Useful for spherical, cylindrical, and planar symmetry
Flux: ΦE = EA cos θ for uniform field and flat surface
50) Capacitors
C = Q/ΔV
Parallel plate: C = εA/d
Energy stored: U = 1/2 CV^2 = 1/2 QV = Q^2/(2C)
Capacitors in parallel: Ceq = ΣC
Capacitors in series: 1/Ceq = Σ(1/C)
With dielectric: C increases by factor κ in ideal case
51) Dielectrics and permittivity
ε = κε0Polarization and bound charge appear in materials
52) Current and resistance
I = dQ/dt
V = IR
R = ρL/A
Conductivity: σ = 1/ρ
Current density: J = I/A
Microscopic form: J = σE
Electrical power: P = IV = I^2R = V^2/R
53) Resistive materials and temperature
R = R0[1 + α(T - T0)]
ρ = ρ0[1 + α(T - T0)]
Metal resistance usually increases with temperature in simple models
54) Direct-current circuits
Series resistors: Req = R1 + R2 + ...
Parallel resistors: 1/Req = 1/R1 + 1/R2 + ...
Kirchhoff junction rule: ΣIin = ΣIout
Kirchhoff loop rule: ΣΔV = 0 around closed loop
Battery with internal resistance: Vterminal = ε - Ir
Power delivered by source: P = εI
55) RC circuits
Time constant: τ = RC
Charging capacitor: q(t) = Q(1 - e^-t/RC)
Vc(t) = V(1 - e^-t/RC)
Discharging capacitor: q(t) = Qe^-t/RC
Current during discharge: I(t) = (Q/RC)e^-t/RC
56) Magnetism: force on charge and wire
Magnetic force on charge: F = qvB sin θ
Force on wire: F = ILB sin θ
Direction by right-hand rule
Magnetic force does no work on a point charge because it is perpendicular to velocity
57) Circular motion in magnetic field
qvB = mv^2/r
r = mv/(qB)
ω = qB/m
T = 2πm/(qB)
v = perpendicular component to B for circular path
58) Magnetic field sources
Long straight wire: B = μ0I/(2πr)
Center of circular loop: B = μ0I/(2R)
Solenoid: B ≈ μ0nI
Toroid: B = μ0NI/(2πr) in ideal region
μ0 = permeability of free space
59) Ampère’s law and magnetic flux
∮ B · dl = μ0Ienc
Magnetic flux: ΦB = ∫ B · dA
For uniform field: ΦB = BA cos θ
60) Faraday’s law and induction
ε = -dΦB/dt
N-turn coil: ε = -N dΦB/dt
Lenz’s law: induced current opposes change in flux
Induced emf from moving rod: ε = BLv if perpendicular and ideal geometry
Transformer idea: Vp/Vs = Np/Ns
61) Inductors and RL circuits
Induced emf in inductor: εL = -L dI/dt
Energy stored in inductor: U = 1/2 LI^2
Time constant: τ = L/R
Current growth in RL: I(t) = (V/R)(1 - e^-tR/L) in simple charging case
62) AC circuits
V(t) = Vmax sin(ωt + φ)
Irms = Imax/√2
Vrms = Vmax/√2
Reactance of capacitor: XC = 1/(ωC)
Reactance of inductor: XL = ωL
Impedance: Z = √(R^2 + (XL - XC)^2)
Imax = Vmax/Z
Average power: Pavg = Vrms Irms cos φ
63) Resonance
Resonance occurs when driving frequency matches natural frequency
In RLC circuits, resonance often when XL = XC
Then Z is minimum for series RLC, current is maximum
64) Electromagnetic waves
c = λf
c = 1/√(μ0ε0)
E = cB for plane EM waves
Energy transport is given by Poynting vector conceptually
Intensity proportional to square of field amplitude
65) Relativity basics
γ = 1/√(1 - v^2/c^2)
Time dilation: Δt = γΔt0
Length contraction: L = L0/γ
Relativistic momentum: p = γmv
Relativistic energy: E = γmc^2
Rest energy: E0 = mc^2
Total energy: E^2 = (pc)^2 + (mc^2)^2
66) Photoelectric effect
hf = φ + Kmax
Kmax = hf - φ
Stopping potential: eVs = Kmax
Threshold frequency: f0 = φ/h
Work function: φ
67) Matter waves and quantum mechanics
de Broglie wavelength: λ = h/p
Heisenberg uncertainty: Δx Δp ≥ ħ/2
ΔE Δt ≥ ħ/2
h = Planck’s constant
ħ = h/(2π)
68) Bohr model and hydrogen-like atoms
En = -13.6 eV / n^2 for hydrogen
Photon energy: ΔE = hf = hc/λ
Rydberg formula: 1/λ = R(1/n1^2 - 1/n2^2), n2 > n1
Angular momentum quantization: L = nħ
69) Nuclear physics
Nuclear binding energy: EB = [ZmH + (A-Z)mn - m nucleus]c^2
Mass defect: Δm = sum of free masses - nucleus mass
Radioactive decay: N = N0 e^-λt
Activity: A = λN
Half-life: t1/2 = ln 2 / λ
Mean life: τ = 1/λ
70) Nuclear reactions and decay
Alpha decay reduces mass number by 4 and atomic number by 2
Beta-minus decay increases atomic number by 1
Beta-plus decay decreases atomic number by 1
Gamma emission changes energy state, not Z or A
Q-value = initial rest energy - final rest energy
71) Useful constants
g ≈ 9.8 m/s^2
c ≈ 3.00 × 10^8 m/s
G ≈ 6.67 × 10^-11 N·m^2/kg^2
k = 8.99 × 10^9 N·m^2/C^2
R = 8.314 J/(mol·K)
NA = 6.022 × 10^23 mol^-1
e = 1.602 × 10^-19 C
h = 6.626 × 10^-34 J·s
ħ = 1.055 × 10^-34 J·s
72) Useful approximation formulas
sin θ ≈ θ for small θ in radians
tan θ ≈ θ for small θ in radians
cos θ ≈ 1 - θ^2/2 for small θ
1/(1+x) ≈ 1 - x for |x| << 1
(1+x)^n ≈ 1 + nx for |x| << 1
73) Graph interpretation in physics
Slope of x-t graph = velocity
Slope of v-t graph = acceleration
Area under v-t graph = displacement
Area under a-t graph = change in velocity
Slope of F-x graph = stiffness for spring-like system in some contexts
Area under F-x graph = work
74) Error and uncertainty
Absolute error = |measured - true|
Relative error = absolute error / true value
Percent uncertainty = relative error × 100%
Addition/subtraction: absolute uncertainties add
Multiplication/division: relative uncertainties add
Power rule: relative uncertainty is multiplied by exponent
75) Experimental data fitting
Best-fit line: y = mx + b
Slope from data often estimates physical constant
Intercept often represents offset or systematic error
Linearization examples: y = kx, y = k/x, y = mx + b after transformation
76) Common physics identities and reminders
Do not mix up mass and weight: m is kg, weight is N
Energy units match work units: J
Power is rate of energy transfer: W = J/s
Momentum is vector
Work is scalar
Torque is vector-like with direction given by right-hand rule
Field quantities often obey superposition
77) Summary relations tying topics together
Newton: ΣF = ma
Energy: Wnet = ΔK
Momentum: J = Δp
Rotation: τ = Iα
Fluids: P = F/A and P = P0 + ρgh
Thermo: ΔU = Q - W
Waves: v = fλ
Electricity: V = IR
Magnetism: F = qvB sin θ
Modern: E = mc^2 and λ = h/p
78) End of study bank
Use the formulas by topic order: math → mechanics → fluids → thermo → oscillations and waves → optics → electricity and magnetism → modern physics
79) Vector calculus identities
grad f = ∇f
div F = ∇·F
curl F = ∇×F
∇²f = d²f/dx² + d²f/dy² + d²f/dz²
∇·(∇×F) = 0
∇×(∇f) = 0
Gauss theorem: ∭(∇·F)dV = ∬F·dA
Stokes theorem: ∬(∇×F)·dA = ∮F·dl
80) Common differential equations
First order linear: dy/dx + P(x)y = Q(x)
Separable form: dy/g(y) = f(x)dx
Exponential growth/decay: dN/dt = ±kN
Harmonic oscillator: x'' + ω²x = 0
Damped oscillator: x'' + 2βx' + ω₀²x = 0
Driven oscillator: x'' + 2βx' + ω₀²x = (F₀/m)cos ωt
Characteristic equation: r² + 2βr + ω₀² = 0
81) Complex numbers and phasors
i² = -1
z = a + ib
|z| = √(a² + b²)
z = re^(iθ) = r(cos θ + i sin θ)
Euler: e^(iθ) = cos θ + i sin θ
Arg(z) = tan^-1(b/a)
Phasor derivative: d/dt[Ae^(iωt)] = iωAe^(iωt)
Impedance in phasor form: V = IZ
82) Sequences and series
Arithmetic sum: S_n = n(a1 + an)/2
Geometric sum: S_n = a(1-r^n)/(1-r)
Infinite geometric sum: S = a/(1-r), |r|<1
Binomial theorem: (a+b)^n = Σ C(n,k)a^(n-k)b^k
Taylor series: f(x) = Σ f^(n)(a)(x-a)^n/n!
Maclaurin: f(x) = f(0) + f'(0)x + f''(0)x²/2! + ...
83) Matrices and determinants
AB = matrix product
|A| = determinant of A
A^-1 exists when |A| ≠ 0
2x2 inverse: [[a,b],[c,d]]^-1 = (1/(ad-bc))[[d,-b],[-c,a]]
Trace: tr(A) = Σ a_ii
Eigenvalue equation: A v = λ v
Characteristic polynomial: det(A - λI) = 0
Rank = number of independent rows or columns
84) Probability and statistics
Mean μ = Σxi/n
Variance σ² = Σ(xi-μ)²/n
Sample variance s² = Σ(xi-x̄)²/(n-1)
Standard deviation σ = √variance
Cov(X,Y) = E[(X-μx)(Y-μy)]
Correlation r = cov/(σxσy)
Bayes: P(A|B)=P(B|A)P(A)/P(B)
Expected value: E[X] = Σ xi pi
85) Coordinate transformations
Cartesian to polar: x = r cos θ, y = r sin θ
r = √(x²+y²), θ = tan^-1(y/x)
Cylindrical: x = r cos θ, y = r sin θ, z = z
Spherical: x = r sin θ cos φ, y = r sin θ sin φ, z = r cos θ
Line element in polar: ds² = dr² + r²dθ²
Velocity in polar: v = ṙ er + rθ̇ eθ
86) Rigid body kinematics
v = ω × r
a = α × r + ω × (ω × r)
For planar rotation: vt = ωr
Normal acceleration: an = ω²r
Angular momentum: L = Iω for principal-axis motion
Rotational kinetic energy: K = 1/2 Iω²
Rolling constraint: vcm = ωR
Instantaneous center of rotation concept
87) Torque, equilibrium, and statics
ΣF = 0 for translational equilibrium
Στ = 0 for rotational equilibrium
Torque magnitude: τ = rF sin θ
Lever arm method: τ = F d⊥
Center of mass balance: Σmi ri / Σmi
Two-force member: force acts along the line joining forces
Beam equilibrium: ΣFx=0, ΣFy=0, Στ=0
88) Stress, strain, and elasticity
Normal stress: σ = F/A
Shear stress: τ = F/A
Normal strain: ε = ΔL/L0
Young's modulus: Y = stress/strain
Bulk modulus: K = -ΔP/(ΔV/V)
Shear modulus: G = shear stress/shear strain
Hooke rod extension: ΔL = FL/(AY)
Strain energy density = 1/2 σ ε
89) Bending and torsion
Flexure formula: σ = My/I
Beam curvature: 1/R = M/(EI)
Second moment of area I depends on geometry
Torsion in circular shaft: τ = Tr/J
Angle of twist: θ = TL/(JG)
Polar moment J = ∫r² dA
Hollow shaft: J = (π/2)(ro^4 - ri^4)
Maximum shear in shaft at outer radius
90) Viscosity and laminar flow
Shear stress: τ = η dv/dy
Poiseuille: Q = πr^4ΔP/(8ηL)
Terminal velocity sphere: vt ∝ r²(ρs-ρf)g/η
Reynolds number: Re = ρvD/η
Stokes drag: Fd = 6πηrv
Laminar flow profile in pipe is parabolic
Dynamic viscosity η; kinematic viscosity ν = η/ρ
91) Advanced fluids
Bernoulli with losses: P1 + 1/2ρv1² + ρgy1 = P2 + 1/2ρv2² + ρgy2 + losses
Momentum flux changes create thrust
Continuity for incompressible flow: A v = constant
Hydraulic power: P = ΔP Q
Manometer relation: ΔP = ρgh
Surface pressure jump: ΔP = γ(1/R1 + 1/R2)
Dynamic head: 1/2ρv²
92) Thermal physics extras
Thermal resistance: Rth = ΔT/ḊQ
Series thermal resistances add
Heat equation: ∂T/∂t = α∇²T
Diffusivity: α = k/(ρc)
Newton cooling: dT/dt = -k(T-Tenv)
Wien's law: λmax T = b
Blackbody power: P = σεAT⁴
Emissivity 0≤ε≤1
93) Entropy and statistical thermodynamics
S = kB ln Ω
ΔS = ∫δQrev/T
Gibbs free energy: G = H - TS
Helmholtz free energy: F = U - TS
At equilibrium, free energy is minimized at fixed constraints
Maxwell relation examples follow from equality of mixed derivatives
Partition function: Z = Σ e^(-Ei/kBT)
Average energy: = -∂ln Z/∂β
94) Gas kinetic theory
P = 1/3 n m vrms²
vrms = √(3kBT/m)
Mean free path λmfp = 1/(√2 π d² n)
Collision frequency increases with density
Equipartition: each quadratic degree contributes 1/2 kBT
Internal energy of ideal monatomic gas: U = 3/2 nRT
Maxwell speed distribution describes molecular speeds
95) Oscillations and resonance
x = A e^(-βt) cos(ωdt + φ)
ωd = √(ω₀² - β²)
Quality factor: Q = ω₀/(2β)
Bandwidth Δω ≈ ω₀/Q
Resonance amplitude peaks near driving frequency
Phase lag changes through resonance
Energy decay time ∝ 1/β
Forced response depends on damping
96) Waves on strings and membranes
String speed: v = √(T/μ)
Transverse waves satisfy y'' = (1/v²) y_tt
Standing wave nodes separated by λ/2
Drum membrane modes depend on geometry
Wave intensity ∝ A²
Power on string: Pavg = 1/2 μω²A²v
Superposition forms beats and envelopes
Group velocity = dω/dk
97) Wave packets and dispersion
Phase velocity: vp = ω/k
Group velocity: vg = dω/dk
Dispersion occurs when vg depends on k
Gaussian packet spreads in dispersive media
Fourier decomposition: signal = Σ components
Uncertainty in x and k related by packet width
modulation/envelope carries information
98) Geometrical optics extras
Snell: n1 sin θ1 = n2 sin θ2
Lens maker: 1/f = (n-1)(1/R1 - 1/R2)
Magnification for thin lens: m = -di/do
Two-lens system: equivalent power approximately additive for thin lenses in contact
Prism deviation depends on refractive index and apex angle
Optical path length: OP = ∫ n ds
Focal ratio = f/D
99) Interference and diffraction
Double slit fringe spacing: Δy = λL/d
Path difference: Δ = d sin θ
Single slit minima: a sin θ = mλ
Grating equation: d sin θ = mλ
Resolving power: R = λ/Δλ
Thin film constructive condition depends on phase reversals
Coherence length limits observable fringes
100) Polarization and birefringence
Malus: I = I0 cos² θ
Unpolarized light after polarizer: I = I0/2
Brewster angle: tan θB = n2/n1
Ordinary and extraordinary rays in anisotropic media
Retarder phase shift: δ = 2πΔn t/λ
Quarter-wave plate: δ = π/2
Half-wave plate: δ = π
Polarization state can be represented by Jones vectors
101) Electric potential and field energy
E = -∇V
ΔV = -∫ E·dl
U = qV
Energy density in electric field: ue = 1/2 εE²
Potential of dipole depends on orientation and distance
Work by field = -ΔU
Superposition applies to potentials as scalars
102) Capacitance networks and dielectrics
Series capacitor rule: 1/Ceq = Σ1/Ci
Parallel capacitor rule: Ceq = ΣCi
Energy in a capacitor: U = 1/2 CV²
Dielectric constant κ = C/C0
Field reduced inside ideal dielectric by factor κ in simple models
Polarization P produces bound charge
Surface charge density on plates: σ = Q/A
103) Current, resistance, and circuits
I = dQ/dt
V = IR
R = ρL/A
Power: P = IV = I²R = V²/R
Kirchhoff junction rule: ΣI = 0 at node
Kirchhoff loop rule: ΣΔV = 0 around loop
Internal resistance: Vterminal = ε - Ir
Electrical energy delivered: W = QV
104) Circuit theorems
Thevenin equivalent: Vth in series with Rth
Norton equivalent: In in parallel with Rn
Maximum power transfer when load resistance equals source resistance in DC linear networks
Superposition theorem for linear circuits
Node-voltage analysis from KCL
Mesh-current analysis from KVL
Delta-wye conversion formulas convert resistive networks
105) RC transients
Charging: q = Q(1 - e^(-t/RC))
Vc = V(1 - e^(-t/RC))
i = (V/R)e^(-t/RC)
Discharging: q = Qe^(-t/RC)
Current decay: i = (Q/RC)e^(-t/RC)
Time constant τ = RC
At t = τ, charge reaches 63.2% in charging
At 5τ, response is nearly complete
106) RL and RLC circuits
RL time constant: τ = L/R
Current growth: i = (V/R)(1 - e^(-Rt/L))
Inductor voltage: VL = L di/dt
Series RLC resonance when XL = XC
ω0 = 1/√(LC)
Z = √(R² + (XL - XC)²)
Bandwidth increases with damping
Energy oscillates between L and C
107) AC circuits and power
v = Vmax sin(ωt + φ)
i = Imax sin(ωt + φi)
Vrms = Vmax/√2
Irms = Imax/√2
XC = 1/(ωC)
XL = ωL
Average power: Pavg = Vrms Irms cos φ
Power factor = cos φ
108) Magnetic forces and fields
F = qvB sin θ
F = ILB sin θ
B straight wire = μ0I/(2πr)
B center loop = μ0I/(2R)
B solenoid ≈ μ0nI
B toroid = μ0NI/(2πr)
Magnetic dipole torque: τ = μ × B
Dipole potential energy: U = -μ·B
109) Electromagnetic induction
ΦB = ∫ B·dA
Faraday: ε = -dΦB/dt
N turns: ε = -N dΦB/dt
Lenz law opposes flux change
Motional emf: ε = BLv
Transformer ratio: Vs/Vp = Ns/Np
Ideal transformer power conserved approximately: VpIp = VsIs
Self inductance: ε = -L di/dt
110) Electromagnetic waves
c = λf
c = 1/√(μ0ε0)
E = cB for plane wave
Intensity ∝ E² ∝ B²
Poynting vector S = (1/μ0) E × B
Radiation pressure depends on absorption/reflection
EM waves are transverse
In a vacuum, E and B are perpendicular to propagation
111) Relativity and spacetime
γ = 1/√(1 - v²/c²)
Δt = γΔt0
L = L0/γ
p = γmv
E = γmc²
Rest energy E0 = mc²
E² = (pc)² + (mc²)²
Relativity of simultaneity affects time ordering
112) Photoelectric and photon physics
hf = φ + Kmax
Kmax = hf - φ
Stopping potential: eVs = Kmax
Threshold frequency f0 = φ/h
Photon momentum p = h/λ
Photon energy E = hf = hc/λ
Intensity controls number of photons, not individual photon energy
113) Matter waves and uncertainty
λ = h/p
Δx Δp ≥ ħ/2
ΔE Δt ≥ ħ/2
Wavefunction normalization: ∫|ψ|² dV = 1
Probability density = |ψ|²
Expectation value: = ∫ψ* A ψ dV
Operator momentum: p̂ = -iħ∇
Energy operator: Ĥψ = iħ ∂ψ/∂t
114) Bohr atom and hydrogen spectrum
En = -13.6 eV/n² for hydrogen
ΔE = hf = hc/λ
Rydberg: 1/λ = R(1/n1² - 1/n2²)
Angular momentum L = nħ
Radius rn ∝ n²
Transition lines form Lyman, Balmer, Paschen series
Ionization from ground state requires 13.6 eV
115) Nuclear structure and decay
Mass defect: Δm = mfree - mnucleus
Binding energy EB = Δmc²
Radioactive law: N = N0e^(-λt)
Activity A = λN
Half-life t1/2 = ln 2 / λ
Mean life τ = 1/λ
Decay chains can be solved recursively
Nuclear radius R ≈ R0 A^(1/3)
116) Nuclear reactions
Q = (mi - mf)c²
Alpha decay: A decreases by 4, Z decreases by 2
Beta-minus: Z increases by 1
Beta-plus: Z decreases by 1
Gamma emission changes energy state only
Fission releases energy from mass defect
Fusion combines light nuclei at high temperature and pressure
117) Particle physics basics
Charge conservation, baryon number conservation, and lepton number conservation guide reactions
Antiparticles have opposite charge and quantum numbers
Quarks combine to form hadrons
Mesons are quark-antiquark pairs
Baryons are three-quark states
Weak interactions change flavor
Cross section measures interaction probability
118) Solid state and semiconductors
Conductivity σ = nqμ
Resistivity ρ = 1/σ
Intrinsic carrier density ni rises with temperature
p-type: holes are majority carriers
n-type: electrons are majority carriers
Diode equation: I = Is(e^(qV/nkT) - 1)
Band gap Eg separates valence and conduction bands
Fermi level marks occupancy balance
119) Materials and transport
Diffusion flux: J = -D ∇c
Fick's law describes mass transport
Thermal expansion: ΔL = αL0ΔT
Electrical mobility μ = v_d/E
Drift velocity: vd = μE
Hall voltage indicates carrier sign
Permeability and permittivity govern field response
Composite properties depend on volume fraction and geometry
120) Astrophysics and cosmology
Luminosity L = 4πR²σT⁴ for blackbody star
Apparent flux F = L/(4πd²)
Distance modulus relates flux and magnitude
Escape speed: vesc = √(2GM/R)
Virial theorem: 2K + U ≈ 0 for bound systems
Hubble law: v = H0 d
Redshift z = Δλ/λ
Critical density ρc = 3H0²/(8πG)
121) Orbital mechanics extras
Vis-viva: v² = GM(2/r - 1/a)
Specific orbital energy: ε = -GM/(2a)
Angular momentum per unit mass: h = √(GMa(1-e²))
Periapsis and apoapsis depend on eccentricity e
Kepler equation: M = E - e sin E
For circular orbit, a = r and e = 0
Escape trajectory has nonnegative specific energy
122) Geophysics and gravitation
Surface gravity g ≈ GM/R²
Free-fall time depends on density in simple models
Potential energy near surface: U = mgh
Isostasy relates crustal thickness to buoyancy balance
Geostrophic balance: pressure gradient and Coriolis force balance
Gravitational potential difference: ΔV = -∫g·dl
Tidal force scales with inverse cube of distance
123) Classical field equations summary
Newton gravity: ∇·g = -4πGρ
Electrostatics: ∇·E = ρ/ε0
Magnetostatics: ∇·B = 0
Faraday: ∇×E = -∂B/∂t
Ampère-Maxwell: ∇×B = μ0J + μ0ε0 ∂E/∂t
These are Maxwell equations in differential form
124) Mechanical waves and acoustics
Sound speed in gas: v ≈ √(γRT/M)
Intensity I = P/A
Sound level β = 10 log10(I/I0)
I0 = 10^-12 W/m²
Doppler for sound in still air: f' = f(v ± vo)/(v ∓ vs)
Beat frequency = |f1 - f2|
Harmonics create timbre and resonance
125) Standing waves in air columns
Open pipe: λn = 2L/n, fn = nv/(2L)
Closed pipe: λn = 4L/n for odd n only
fn = nv/(4L), n = 1,3,5,...
End correction slightly changes effective length
Nodes at closed ends and antinodes at open ends
Pipe resonances depend on boundary conditions
126) Numerical approximations
sin θ ≈ θ for small θ
cos θ ≈ 1 - θ²/2
e^x ≈ 1 + x for |x| << 1
ln(1+x) ≈ x for |x| << 1
(1+x)^n ≈ 1 + nx when |x| << 1
Finite difference derivative: f'(x) ≈ [f(x+h)-f(x)]/h
Trapezoid rule approximates integrals by area blocks
127) Error propagation
Absolute error = |measured - true|
Relative error = absolute error / true value
Percent error = relative error × 100%
Addition/subtraction: absolute errors add
Multiplication/division: relative errors add
Power rule: relative uncertainty multiplied by exponent
Covariance matters when errors are correlated
128) Dimensional analysis checklist
[speed] = LT^-1
[acceleration] = LT^-2
[force] = MLT^-2
[energy] = ML²T^-2
[power] = ML²T^-3
[pressure] = ML^-1T^-2
[momentum] = MLT^-1
[electric field] = MLT^-3I^-1
Equation terms must have matching dimensions
129) Useful constants and conversions
g ≈ 9.8 m/s²
c ≈ 3.00 × 10^8 m/s
G ≈ 6.67 × 10^-11 N·m²/kg²
k = 8.99 × 10^9 N·m²/C²
ε0 ≈ 8.85 × 10^-12 F/m
μ0 = 4π × 10^-7 N/A²
R = 8.314 J/(mol·K)
h = 6.626 × 10^-34 J·s
ħ = h/(2π)
130) Prefixes and unit relations
k = 10^3
M = 10^6
G = 10^9
T = 10^12
m = 10^-3
μ = 10^-6
n = 10^-9
p = 10^-12
1 N = 1 kg·m/s²
1 J = 1 N·m
1 W = 1 J/s
1 Pa = 1 N/m²
131) Laboratory data and graphing
Best-fit line: y = mx + b
Slope estimates a proportional constant
Intercept often indicates systematic offset
Log-log plot linearizes power laws: y = kx^n
Semi-log plot linearizes exponentials: y = Ae^(bx)
Area under curve often represents accumulated quantity
Residuals show model mismatch
132) Control systems basics
Transfer function G(s) = output/input in Laplace domain
Feedback modifies effective gain
Proportional control: u = Kp e
Integral control removes steady-state error
Derivative control predicts error trend
Closed-loop stability depends on poles
Time constant and damping shape response
133) Laplace transform basics
L{f(t)} = F(s)
L{f'} = sF - f(0)
L{∫f dt} = F/s
L{e^(at)} = 1/(s-a)
L{sin bt} = b/(s²+b²)
L{cos bt} = s/(s²+b²)
Inverse Laplace retrieves time response from s-domain model
134) Fourier analysis
f(t) = ∫ F(ω)e^(iωt)dω
Fourier coefficients describe frequency content
Parseval links time and frequency energy
Bandwidth controls signal detail
Convolution in time becomes multiplication in frequency
Delta function has flat spectrum in ideal limit
Sampling rate must exceed Nyquist frequency
135) Quantum operators and commutators
[x, p] = iħ
Measurement operators are Hermitian
Eigenvalues correspond to possible outcomes
Commutator zero implies simultaneous diagonalization in ideal cases
Angular momentum algebra: [Lx, Ly] = iħLz and cyclic permutations
Uncertainty can be derived from commutators
Time evolution follows Schrödinger equation
136) Angular momentum in quantum mechanics
L² = l(l+1)ħ²
Lz = mħ
l = 0,1,2,...
m = -l,...,+l
Spherical harmonics Ylm encode angular states
Spin adds intrinsic angular momentum
Total angular momentum couples via addition rules
Selection rules constrain transitions
137) Lasers and optical physics
Stimulated emission amplifies coherent light
Population inversion is required for lasing
Gain exceeds losses at threshold
Cavity modes satisfy resonance condition
Laser output can be monochromatic and directional
Photon energy still obeys E = hf
Coherence length is large compared with ordinary light
138) Superconductivity and low-temperature physics
Below critical temperature Tc, resistivity can vanish in ideal models
Meissner effect expels magnetic field from bulk
Critical current and critical field limit superconducting state
Flux quantization: Φ = n(h/2e)
Cooper pairs carry charge 2e
BCS theory explains pairing mechanism in conventional superconductors
Superconducting gap sets excitation scale
139) More trigonometric identities
sin(A±B)=sinA cosB ± cosA sinB
cos(A±B)=cosA cosB ∓ sinA sinB
tan(A±B)=(tanA ± tanB)/(1 ∓ tanA tanB)
sin2θ=2sinθ cosθ
cos2θ=cos²θ-sin²θ=1-2sin²θ=2cos²θ-1
sinθ = 2tan(θ/2)/(1+tan²(θ/2))
140) Inverse trig relations
arcsin x + arccos x = π/2
arctan x + arctan(1/x)=π/2 for x>0
d/dx arctan x = 1/(1+x²)
d/dx arcsin x = 1/√(1-x²)
d/dx arccos x = -1/√(1-x²)
tan(arctan x)=x
141) Hyperbolic functions
sinh x = (e^x-e^-x)/2
cosh x = (e^x+e^-x)/2
tanh x = sinh x/cosh x
cosh²x - sinh²x = 1
d/dx sinh x = cosh x
d/dx cosh x = sinh x
142) Integral formulas
∫x^n dx = x^(n+1)/(n+1)+C
∫e^x dx = e^x + C
∫sin x dx = -cos x + C
∫cos x dx = sin x + C
∫1/x dx = ln|x| + C
∫1/(1+x²) dx = arctan x + C
143) Advanced calculus
d/dx(fg)=f'g+fg'
d/dx(f/g)=(f'g-fg')/g²
Chain rule: d/dx f(g(x)) = f'(g)g'
Integration by parts: ∫u dv = uv - ∫v du
Substitution: ∫f(g(x))g'(x)dx = ∫f(u)du
Fundamental theorem: ∫_a^b f'(x)dx=f(b)-f(a)
144) Taylor and asymptotic expansions
e^x = 1+x+x²/2!+x³/3!+...
sin x = x-x³/3!+x⁵/5!-...
cos x = 1-x²/2!+x⁴/4!-...
ln(1+x)=x-x²/2+x³/3-...
1/(1-x)=1+x+x²+x³+...
For small x, higher-order terms are neglected
145) Matrices in physics
rotation matrix R transforms vectors
R^T R = I for orthogonal matrices
det(R)=1 for proper rotations
Diagonalization simplifies coupled systems
Eigenvectors define principal axes
Mass matrix and stiffness matrix arise in oscillations
146) Coupled oscillations
m x¨ + kx + kc(x-y)=0
m y¨ + ky + kc(y-x)=0
Normal modes have definite frequencies
Symmetric mode x=y
Antisymmetric mode x=-y
Mode frequencies found from determinant condition
147) Small oscillation method
Expand potential near equilibrium: U≈U0+1/2 k_eff x²
Equilibrium from dU/dx = 0
Frequency ω = √(k_eff/m)
Stable equilibrium requires k_eff > 0
Unstable equilibrium has k_eff < 0
Local quadratic approximation predicts period
148) Central force motion
F(r) = -dU/dr
Angular momentum L = mr²θ̇ is conserved
Effective potential: Ueff = U(r) + L²/(2mr²)
Circular orbit from dUeff/dr = 0
Stability from d²Ueff/dr² > 0
Kepler orbits follow inverse-square force
149) Keplerian orbit geometry
r = a(1-e²)/(1+e cos ν)
e = eccentricity, a = semi-major axis
Period: T² = 4π²a³/(GM)
Area law: dA/dt = constant
True anomaly ν locates position
Mean anomaly advances uniformly in time
150) Rocket and variable-mass motion
Thrust T = ṁ ve
Rocket equation: Δv = ve ln(m0/mf)
Mass loss changes acceleration dynamically
Specific impulse Isp = ve/g0
Exhaust speed and mass ratio control performance
Gravity and drag reduce ideal Δv
151) Momentum in 2D collisions
Σpx,i = Σpx,f
Σpy,i = Σpy,f
Elastic collision also conserves kinetic energy
Angle of scattering determined by component conservation
Center-of-mass frame simplifies analysis
Impulse equals area under force-time curve
152) Rotating frames
Coriolis force = -2m(Ω×v')
Centrifugal force = -mΩ×(Ω×r)
Euler force = -m(dΩ/dt×r)
Noninertial frames add fictitious terms
Foucault pendulum precesses due to Earth rotation
Geophysical flows use rotating frame dynamics
153) Planetary and satellite motion
vorb = √(GM/r)
Escape speed = √(2GM/r)
Geostationary orbit period equals Earth's rotation period
Hill sphere estimates gravitational influence region
Tidal locking arises from dissipation
Orbital insertion requires matching speed and direction
154) Thermodynamic cycles
η = Wout/Qin
Carnot: η = 1 - Tc/Th
Otto and Diesel cycles model engines idealized
Work in p-V diagram equals enclosed area
Refrigerator COP = Qc/W
Heat pump COP = Qh/W
155) Entropy change formulas
ΔS = ∫δQrev/T
Ideal gas: ΔS = nCv ln(T2/T1)+nR ln(V2/V1)
At constant pressure: ΔS = nCp ln(T2/T1)
At constant volume: ΔS = nCv ln(T2/T1)
Isothermal ideal gas: ΔS = nR ln(V2/V1)
Second law requires total entropy nondecrease
156) Chemical thermodynamics
ΔG = ΔH - TΔS
Equilibrium when ΔG = 0
Reaction spontaneity often follows ΔG < 0
ΔH = ΔU + PΔV for constant pressure
Chemical potential drives diffusion
Activity and equilibrium constants connect via G
157) Electrochemistry
Ecell = Ecathode - Eanode
ΔG = -nFEcell
Nernst: E = E° - (RT/nF)ln Q
Faraday constant F = NAe
Electrolysis mass ∝ charge passed
Redox balancing conserves charge and atoms
158) Semiconductor devices
p-n junction barrier controls current
I = Is(e^(qV/nkT)-1)
Zener breakdown used for regulation
LED emits photons with Eg ≈ hf
Transistor action amplifies and switches current
Depletion region width varies with bias
159) Photon and radiation relations
E = hf
p = h/λ
I = energy per area per time
Radiance and intensity depend on geometry
Blackbody spectrum peaks at λmax T = b
Photon number flux equals power divided by hf
160) Atomic transitions
ΔE = hf
Selection rules restrict allowed transitions
Orbital quantum numbers determine line spectra
Fine structure and hyperfine splitting add small shifts
Absorption and emission spectra are complementary
Metastable states can delay decay
161) NMR and spin physics
μ = γS
Larmor frequency ω0 = γB0
Spin-1/2 has two Zeeman levels
Precession occurs around applied magnetic field
Relaxation times T1 and T2 describe return to equilibrium
RF pulses tip magnetization
162) Laser cavity modes
mλ/2 = L for standing-wave resonances
Longitudinal mode spacing Δν = c/(2L)
Threshold gain equals cavity loss
Quality factor Q = ν/Δν
Mode locking creates short pulses
Coherence length increases with spectral purity
163) Nonlinear dynamics basics
x' = f(x,t)
Fixed points satisfy f(x*) = 0
Linearization uses derivative near equilibrium
Stability determined by sign of slope
Periodic forcing can create resonance or chaos
Phase portraits show trajectories in state space
164) Logarithms and exponentials
a^x = e^(x ln a)
ln(ab)=ln a + ln b
ln(a/b)=ln a - ln b
ln(a^n)=n ln a
d/dx ln x = 1/x
d/dx a^x = a^x ln a
165) Vector identities
A·(B×C)=B·(C×A)=C·(A×B)
A×(B×C)=B(A·C)-C(A·B)
|A×B| = |A||B|sinθ
|A·B| = |A||B|cosθ
Projection of A on B = (A·B/|B|²)B
Perpendicular component = A - projection
166) Spherical harmonics and fields
Y_l^m(θ,φ) solves angular eigenproblem
Legendre polynomials appear in axisymmetric systems
Multipole expansion organizes field terms
Monopole ~ 1/r
Dipole ~ 1/r²
Quadrupole ~ 1/r³
167) Multipole and dipole approximations
Potential far away approximated by lowest nonzero multipole
Electric dipole moment p = qd
Torque on dipole τ = p×E
Energy U = -p·E
Magnetic dipole torque τ = μ×B
Field lines show orientation but not magnitude directly
168) Dielectric and magnetic materials
P = ε0χe E
M = χm H
ε = ε0(1+χe)
μ = μ0(1+χm)
Bound charges and currents arise from polarization and magnetization
Linear response assumes proportionality to applied fields
169) Magnetic circuits and induction
Reluctance acts like resistance for flux
Magnetomotive force = NI
Flux linkage λ = NΦ
ε = -d(NΦ)/dt
Inductor energy U = 1/2 LI²
Mutual inductance couples two coils
170) Transmission lines and signals
v = 1/√(LC) for ideal line
Characteristic impedance Z0 = √(L/C)
Reflection coefficient Γ = (ZL-Z0)/(ZL+Z0)
Standing waves arise from mismatch
Signal delay depends on line length and velocity
171) Numerical wave methods
Courant condition constrains stability
Finite difference updates approximate PDEs
Spatial grid spacing Δx and time step Δt must be chosen carefully
Dispersion error affects high-frequency waves
Boundary conditions determine reflected waves
172) Dimensional constants revisited
1 eV = 1.602 × 10^-19 J
1 atm ≈ 1.013 × 10^5 Pa
1 bar = 10^5 Pa
1 cal ≈ 4.184 J
1 L = 10^-3 m³
1 T = 1 Wb/m²
173) Energy and work relations
W = ∫ F·ds
Power P = dW/dt
K = 1/2 mv²
Work-energy theorem: Wnet = ΔK
Potential energy differences correspond to conservative work
Efficiency = useful output/input
174) Impulse and momentum theorem
J = ∫ F dt
J = Δp
Average force Favg = Δp/Δt
Impulse-momentum relation useful for impacts
Area under force-time graph gives impulse
System momentum changes only by external impulse
175) Rotational dynamics formulas
τ = Iα
L = Iω
Krot = 1/2 Iω²
Parallel-axis theorem: I = Icm + Md²
Angular impulse changes angular momentum
Point mass I = mr²
176) Gyroscope and precession
τ = dL/dt
Precession rate Ωp ≈ τ/L
Spin angular momentum resists reorientation
Top motion combines spin and precession
Gravity causes slow change in axis direction
177) Stress waves and seismic motion
P-wave longitudinal motion
S-wave transverse motion
Wave speed depends on elastic moduli and density
Seismic intensity decreases with distance
Reflection and refraction occur at layer boundaries
178) Plasma and charged particle motion
Debye length screens fields
Plasma frequency ωp = √(ne²/(mε0))
Gyroradius r = mv⊥/(qB)
ExB drift = E×B/B²
Collective behavior dominates over binary collisions
179) Transport phenomena
Diffusion J = -D∇c
Heat flux q = -k∇T
Momentum flux related to viscosity
Onsager reciprocity links coupled flows in linear regime
Transport coefficients are material properties
180) Continuum mechanics
Stress tensor σij relates force to area direction
Strain tensor εij describes deformation
Constitutive laws connect stress and strain
In incompressible flow, ∇·v = 0
Navier-Stokes: ρ(Dv/Dt)= -∇p + μ∇²v + ρg
181) Navier-Stokes reminders
Dv/Dt = ∂v/∂t + (v·∇)v
Pressure gradient drives acceleration
Viscous term μ∇²v damps shear
Inviscid limit gives Euler equation
Boundary layers form near solid surfaces
182) Acoustic impedance and reflections
Z = ρv for sound in a medium

Standing waves form in resonators
Intensity ratios depend on amplitude squared
183) Optical fiber basics

Attenuation measured in dB/km

184) Information and entropy
H = -Σ p ln p
Shannon entropy measures uncertainty
More outcomes with equal probabilities increase entropy
Channel capacity limits reliable transmission
Compression reduces redundancy
185) Statistical distributions
Gaussian: f(x) ∝ e^(-(x-μ)²/2σ²)
Poisson mean equals variance
Binomial counts successes in n trials
Exponential describes memoryless waiting times
Normal approximation often applies for large samples
186) Least squares fitting
Minimize Σ(yi - (mxi+b))²
Normal equations yield best fit line
Residuals = observed - predicted

Weighted least squares handles unequal uncertainties
187) Unit analysis examples
Pressure = force/area = N/m²

Current density = A/m²
Surface tension = N/m
Momentum flux = N/m²
Elastic modulus = Pa
188) Useful approximations in physics
For x<<1, e^x ≈ 1+x
For x<<1, ln(1+x) ≈ x
For x<<1, (1+x)^n ≈ 1+nx
For small angles in radians, sin x ≈ x and tan x ≈ x
These simplify analytic estimates
189) Special relativity kinematics
t' = γ(t - vx/c²)
x' = γ(x - vt)
y' = y
z' = z
γ = 1/√(1-v²/c²)
Simultaneity depends on frame
190) Relativistic energy and momentum
p = γmv
E = γmc²
K = (γ-1)mc²
E² = (pc)² + (mc²)²
For photon, m = 0 and E = pc
191) Compton and scattering
Δλ = h/(mec)(1-cos θ)

192) Wavefunctions and normalization

∫|ψ|²dx = 1

Normalization constant makes total probability unity
193) Particle in a box

n = 1,2,3,...

Energy levels increase as n²
194) Harmonic oscillator quantum model

195) Tunneling and barriers

196) Hydrogen atom quantum numbers

197) Perturbation and approximation

200) Stellar structure

201) Stellar evolution

202) Black holes and compact objects

203) General relativity basics

204) Geodesy and Earth data

205) Atmospheric physics

206) Climate energy balance

207) Plasma waves

220) Calculus of variations

221) Hamiltonian mechanics

222) PDEs in physics

223) Spherical coordinates

225) Numerical integration

234) Quantum statistics

236) Phonons and lattice vibrations

241) Advanced kinematics toolbox

254) Differential calculus toolbox

255) Integral calculus toolbox

256) Linear algebra toolbox

orthogonal matrix satisfies R^TR=I

257) Probability toolbox

258) Statistics toolbox

263) Energy equivalences

264) Log and exp review
e^x ≈ 1+x for small x

a^x = e^(x ln a)
d/dx e^x = e^x
265) Small-angle review
sin θ ≈ θ
tan θ ≈ θ

266) Core field equations
∇·E = ρ/ε0
∇·B = 0

These are Maxwell equations
Gravity analog: ∇·g = -4πGρ
267) Mechanical identities
Wnet = ΔK

269) Wave identities
v = fλ

270) Closing revision set
Math supports mechanics, mechanics supports fluids, fluids supports thermodynamics, thermodynamics supports waves, waves support optics, optics supports E&M, and E&M supports modern physics
271) Differential equations toolkit
Linear ODE: y' + P(x)y = Q(x)
Integrating factor: μ(x) = e^(∫P(x)dx)
Then (μy)' = μQ
Second-order homogeneous: ay'' + by' + cy = 0
Characteristic equation: ar² + br + c = 0
Overdamped, critically damped, underdamped cases follow from roots
272) Boundary value problems
Dirichlet boundary: value fixed at boundary
Neumann boundary: derivative fixed at boundary
Robin boundary: a u + b ∂u/∂n = c
Separation ansatz: u(x,t) = X(x)T(t)
Eigenvalues arise from boundary conditions
Orthogonality of modes simplifies expansion
273) Series and convergence tests
Ratio test: lim |a_{n+1}/a_n| = L
If L < 1, series converges absolutely
Root test: lim √[n]{|a_n|} = L
Alternating series converges if terms decrease to 0
Comparison test: compare with known convergent/divergent series
Integral test relates series to improper integrals
274) Power series operations
Differentiate term-by-term within radius of convergence
Integrate term-by-term within radius of convergence
Product of series via Cauchy product
Maclaurin series centered at x = 0
Radius of convergence found by ratio or root test
Analytic functions equal their local power series
275) Multivariable calculus
Partial derivative: ∂f/∂x
Gradient: ∇f = (∂f/∂x, ∂f/∂y, ∂f/∂z)
Total differential: df = f_x dx + f_y dy + f_z dz
Chain rule for multivariable composition
Hessian matrix contains second derivatives
Critical points found from ∇f = 0
276) Optimization with constraints
Lagrangian: L = f(x,y,...) - λg(x,y,...)
Set ∇f = λ∇g at constrained extrema
Multiple constraints require multiple multipliers
Feasible set must satisfy all constraints
Second-derivative tests can classify the stationary point
Boundary checking is essential
277) Calculus in polar coordinates
dA = r dr dθ
Area of region = ∬ r dr dθ
Gradient in polar has radial and angular parts
Velocity: v = ṙ e_r + rθ̇ e_θ
Acceleration: a = (r̈ - rθ̇²)e_r + (rθ̈ + 2ṙθ̇)e_θ
Used for central-force and rotating systems
278) Divergence and curl applications
∇·F measures source strength
∇×F measures local rotation
Conservative field: ∇×F = 0 in simply connected regions
Solenoidal field: ∇·F = 0
Potential flow in fluids uses curl-free velocity field
In electrostatics, ∇×E = 0
279) Green’s theorem and related tools
∮(P dx + Q dy) = ∬(∂Q/∂x - ∂P/∂y)dA
Useful for planar circulation and area computations
Flux form uses outward normal integral
Transforms line integrals into double integrals
Requires appropriate smoothness conditions
Foundation for later vector theorems
280) Fourier transform relations
F(ω) = ∫ f(t)e^(-iωt)dt
Inverse: f(t) = (1/2π)∫ F(ω)e^(iωt)dω
Time shift gives phase factor
Frequency shift gives modulation
Convolution in time becomes multiplication in frequency
Parseval’s relation preserves energy
281) Laplace transform relations
ℒ{f(t)} = F(s)
ℒ{f'} = sF - f(0)
ℒ{f''} = s²F - s f(0) - f'(0)
Useful for initial-value problems
Heaviside step functions simplify switched circuits
Impulse response gives system response by convolution
282) Nonlinear systems and stability
Fixed point satisfies f(x) = 0
Linearize: x' ≈ f'(x)(x - x)
If f'(x) < 0, fixed point is stable in 1D
Nullclines identify equilibria in 2D systems
Phase portraits show trajectories
Bifurcations occur when parameter changes alter stability
283) Friction and contact mechanics
Static friction: fs ≤ μs N
Kinetic friction: fk = μk N
Friction direction opposes relative motion or tendency to move
Angle of repose related to μs
Dry friction often modeled as approximately speed-independent
Rolling friction is usually much smaller than sliding friction
284) Drag and terminal speed
Linear drag: Fd = bv
Quadratic drag: Fd = cv²
Terminal speed occurs when net force = 0
For falling body with linear drag, v(t) approaches vt exponentially
For quadratic drag, approach to vt is nonlinear
Drag coefficient depends on shape and Reynolds number
285) Elastic collisions in 1D
Momentum conserved: m1u1 + m2u2 = m1v1 + m2v2
Kinetic energy conserved: 1/2 m1u1² + 1/2 m2u2² = 1/2 m1v1² + 1/2 m2v2²
Relative speed of separation equals relative speed of approach
Useful closed-form solutions exist for v1, v2
Special case m1 = m2 gives velocity exchange
286) Rotational statics and center of mass
x_cm = Σmixi / Σmi
y_cm = Σmiyi / Σmi
z_cm = Σmizi / Σmi
Balance requires support line through projection of center of mass
Torque from distributed load can be integrated
Equivalent single force acts at center of pressure in some contexts
287) Gyroscopic and precessional motion
L = Iω for symmetric spinning body
τ = dL/dt
Precession rate Ωp ≈ τ/L
Fast spin stabilizes orientation
Nutation is the oscillatory component of motion
Gyroscope behavior depends on spin axis and applied torque
288) Orbital transfers and maneuvers
Hohmann transfer uses two tangential burns
Transfer ellipse touches initial and final circular orbits
Burn 1 raises apogee or perigee
Burn 2 circularizes at destination orbit
Δv budget is additive across maneuvers
Plane change cost increases with orbital speed
289) Relativistic kinematics extensions
Momentum: p = γmv
Energy: E = γmc²
Kinetic energy: K = (γ - 1)mc²
Invariant interval: s² = c²t² - x² - y² - z²
Proper time dτ = dt/γ
Four-velocity and four-momentum simplify covariant formulas
290) Doppler formulas for light
Relativistic Doppler shift: f' = f√((1 + β)/(1 - β)) for approaching motion
Redshift: z = (λ' - λ)/λ
For small speeds, Δf/f ≈ ±v/c
Motion along line of sight dominates first-order shift
Used in astronomy and radar systems
291) Blackbody radiation laws
Planck spectrum: B(λ,T) from quantized photon distribution
Stefan-Boltzmann: P/A = σT⁴ for ideal blackbody
Wien displacement: λmaxT = b
Higher T shifts peak to shorter wavelength
Emissivity modifies ideal blackbody power
Thermal radiation depends strongly on temperature
292) Heat engines and entropy production
Efficient cycle requires small temperature difference in heat exchange
Entropy generation is nonnegative in real processes
Irreversible work loss reduces useful output
Exergy measures maximum useful work relative to environment
Heat rejection unavoidable for cyclic engines
Carnot limit is only achieved by reversible cycle
293) Chemical potential and phase equilibrium
At phase equilibrium, chemical potentials are equal
μ1 = μ2 across coexisting phases
Gibbs free energy minimized at fixed T, P, composition
Clausius-Clapeyron slope: dP/dT = L/(TΔv)
Phase diagrams locate coexistence lines and critical point
Latent heat appears in first-order transitions
294) Capillary and wetting phenomena
Young’s equation: γSV - γSL = γLV cos θ
Contact angle θ controls wetting behavior
Capillary rise depends on γ, ρ, g, r, and θ
Smaller radius increases rise height
Complete wetting gives very small contact angle
Nonwetting surfaces give large contact angle
295) Compressible flow basics
Mach number Ma = v/c
Subsonic: Ma < 1
Supersonic: Ma > 1
Shock waves form in supersonic flow
Isentropic relations link pressure, temperature, and density changes
Nozzle flow can choke at Mach 1
296) Rigid body energy and angular impulse
Rotational work: W = ∫τ dθ
Angular impulse: ∫τ dt = ΔL
Power in rotation: P = τω
Rolling motion combines translational and rotational energy
For pure rolling, static friction may do no net work
Energy methods often simplify rolling problems
297) Noninertial forces summary
In rotating frame: fictitious forces are added
Coriolis force = -2mΩ×v'
Centrifugal force = -mΩ×(Ω×r)
Euler force = -m(dΩ/dt × r)
Apparent weights vary in accelerating frames
Used in meteorology and rotating machinery
298) Beam bending and structural response
Stress from bending: σ = My/I
Neutral axis has zero normal stress in ideal beam theory
Deflection relates to curvature of beam
EI sets flexural rigidity
Shear force and bending moment diagrams summarize internal loads
Maximum stress often occurs at extreme fibers
299) Modes in strings and membranes
String harmonics: fn = n v/(2L)
Fixed-fixed string has nodes at both ends
Open membrane modes depend on geometry
Resonant frequencies determine standing-wave patterns
Mode shapes are orthogonal under appropriate inner product
Energy distribution depends on mode amplitudes
300) Radiation transport
Intensity decays as I = I0e^(-μx)
Optical depth τ = μx in uniform medium
Scattering and absorption both contribute to attenuation
Mean free path = 1/μ
Radiative transfer equation governs intensity evolution
Important in astrophysics, shielding, and imaging
301) Plasma frequency and shielding
Plasma frequency: ωp = √(ne²/(mε0))
Debye length: λD = √(ε0kBT/(ne²))
Quasi-neutrality holds over distances larger than λD
Fields are screened in plasma
Collective oscillations dominate over simple binary motion
Magnetized plasmas support anisotropic transport
302) Semiconductor carrier statistics
Electron concentration n and hole concentration p depend on doping and temperature
Mass action law: np = ni² in equilibrium
Intrinsic level lies near band gap center in simple models
Fermi-Dirac distribution governs occupancy
Conductivity: σ = q(nμn + pμp)
Mobility controls transport strength
303) Diode and transistor equations
Diode current: I = Is(e^(qV/nkT) - 1)
Forward bias lowers barrier height
Reverse bias widens depletion region
BJT current gain: β = Ic/Ib
FET channel conductivity controlled by gate voltage
Device models approximate circuit behavior
304) Hall effect and magnetotransport
Hall voltage: VH = IB/(nqt)
Sign of Hall coefficient identifies carrier sign
Lorentz force deflects carriers sideways
Useful for measuring density and mobility
Magnetoresistance changes with magnetic field
Transport becomes tensorial in strong fields
305) Superposition in electromagnetism
E_total = ΣEi
B_total = ΣBi
Potentials add linearly in electrostatics and magnetostatics
Field from continuous distribution found by integration
Symmetry can eliminate components by cancellation
Complex geometries often decomposed into simpler sources
306) Circuit response in frequency domain
Impedance of resistor: ZR = R
Impedance of capacitor: ZC = 1/(iωC)
Impedance of inductor: ZL = iωL
Series combination: Z = ΣZk
Phasor current: I = V/Z
Frequency response reveals filtering behavior
307) Filter characteristics
Low-pass passes low frequencies
High-pass passes high frequencies
Band-pass passes a frequency window
Cutoff frequency defined by -3 dB point in many systems
RC cutoff: fc = 1/(2πRC)
RL cutoff: fc = R/(2πL)
308) Electromagnetic boundary conditions
Tangential E continuous across interface if no time-varying magnetic sheet
Normal B continuous across interface
Normal D jumps by free surface charge
Tangential H jumps by surface current
Boundary conditions determine reflection and transmission
Used in optics and waveguides
309) Waveguide and cavity resonance
Standing waves fit dimensions of cavity
Resonant frequencies depend on geometry and boundary conditions
Cutoff frequency exists for waveguides
Modes labeled by indices describing field variation
Quality factor measures stored versus lost energy
Cavity resonance supports narrowband selectivity
310) Antenna fundamentals
Radiated power depends on current distribution
Effective aperture relates received power to incident intensity
Resonant length often near λ/2 or λ/4
Pattern describes angular radiation intensity
Polarization affects reception efficiency
Impedance matching improves transfer
311) Signal-to-noise and detection
SNR = signal power / noise power
Decibel form: SNRdB = 10 log10(SNR)
Averaging reduces random noise roughly by √N
Threshold detection depends on desired false-alarm rate
Noise bandwidth affects measurement precision
Filters can improve detectability
312) Probability in physical measurements
Count processes often follow Poisson statistics
Poisson variance equals mean
Binomial model applies to repeated yes/no trials
Gaussian approximation emerges for large counts
Measurement uncertainty scales with sample size
Confidence interval width decreases with more data
313) Error analysis and calibration
Bias is systematic offset from truth
Precision describes repeatability
Accuracy describes closeness to truth
Calibration curve maps instrument output to physical quantity
Zero offset and gain error are common corrections
Propagation of uncertainty follows sensitivity coefficients
314) Units and derived dimensions
Velocity: LT^-1
Acceleration: LT^-2
Force: MLT^-2
Energy: ML²T^-2
Power: ML²T^-3
Pressure: ML^-1T^-2
Charge: IT
Voltage: ML²T^-3I^-1
315) Harmonic oscillator solution forms
Undamped: x = A cos(ωt + φ)
Damped: x = Ae^(-βt) cos(ωdt + φ)
Driven steady-state response has phase lag
Quality factor Q = ω0/(2β)
Resonance near driving frequency close to ω0
Amplitude depends on forcing and damping
316) Normal modes in coupled systems
Assume trial motion x = A cos ωt, y = B cos ωt
Mode frequencies come from determinant condition
Symmetric and antisymmetric modes are common
Orthogonal modes simplify energy decomposition
General motion is superposition of modes
Coupled oscillators model molecules and structures
317) Molecular rotational spectra
Rigid rotor energy: E_J = BJ(J+1)
J = 0,1,2,...
Transition selection rule: ΔJ = ±1
Spectral lines equally spaced in ideal rigid-rotor model
Moment of inertia affects line spacing
Microwave spectroscopy probes rotational states
318) Vibrational spectra
Harmonic oscillator levels: En = (n + 1/2)ħω
Selection rule often Δn = ±1 in ideal model
Anharmonicity shifts higher levels
IR activity depends on dipole moment change
Vibrational frequency depends on reduced mass and bond stiffness
Useful in molecular identification
319) Rotational-vibrational coupling
Vibrational and rotational motions can combine
Energy approximated as sum of vibrational and rotational parts
Fine structure appears in spectral bands
Selection rules constrain observed lines
Centrifugal distortion modifies ideal rigid-rotor spectrum
Real molecules deviate from simple models
320) Quantum tunneling through barriers
Wavefunction decays exponentially in forbidden region
Transmission probability increases with lower and thinner barriers
Approximate decay constant depends on barrier height and particle energy
Important in alpha decay and semiconductor junctions
Nonclassical penetration is central to quantum transport
321) Alpha, beta, and gamma decay bookkeeping
Alpha: A → A - 4, Z → Z - 2
Beta-minus: Z → Z + 1, A unchanged
Beta-plus: Z → Z - 1, A unchanged
Gamma emission changes energy state only
Conservation of charge, nucleon number, and energy applies
Decay chains may involve multiple steps
322) Nuclear reaction energetics
Q = (m_initial - m_final)c²
If Q > 0, reaction releases energy
If Q < 0, reaction requires input energy
Threshold effects matter for endothermic reactions
Cross section measures likelihood of a reaction
Breeding and fission depend on nuclear energetics
323) Fission and fusion conditions
Fission of heavy nuclei can release energy from increased binding per nucleon
Fusion of light nuclei can also release energy
Coulomb barrier must be overcome for fusion
High temperature and confinement help fusion
Chain reaction possible in certain fissile materials
Binding energy curve explains both processes
324) Particle kinematics in accelerators
Momentum from curvature: p = qBr for circular motion in magnetic field
Energy gain from electric potential: ΔE = qΔV
High-energy particles approach relativistic regime
Synchrotron radiation becomes important for light particles
Beam optics uses focusing and bending elements
Momentum resolution depends on field and geometry
325) Detector response and counting statistics
Count rate fluctuations often follow √N behavior
Dead time reduces observed rate at high flux
Efficiency = detected / incident
Resolution may be energy, time, or spatial
Background subtraction improves signal extraction
Calibration with known sources is essential
326) Cosmological redshift and expansion
z = (λobs - λem)/λem
For nonrelativistic recession, v ≈ H0d
Scale factor a(t) grows with cosmic expansion
Wavelength stretches with expansion: λ ∝ a
Hubble law links distance and recession speed
Redshift informs distance and lookback time
327) Friedmann-like expansion relations
Expansion rate depends on matter, radiation, curvature, and dark-energy content in standard cosmology
Critical density sets flatness scale
Density parameters sum near unity in flat models
Proper distance evolves with scale factor
Cosmic age depends on expansion history
Accelerated expansion occurs when dark energy dominates
328) Stellar magnitude relations
m1 - m2 = -2.5 log10(F1/F2)
Absolute magnitude compares intrinsic brightness at standard distance
Distance modulus connects apparent and absolute magnitude
Luminosity and flux related by inverse-square law
Magnitude scale is logarithmic
Useful for comparing astronomical objects
329) Astrophysical equilibrium
Hydrostatic balance: dP/dr = -Gmρ/r²
Energy generation balances radiative and convective loss in stars
Radiative gradient depends on opacity and luminosity
Degenerate matter can support compact stars
Pressure support may be thermal, radiation, or degeneracy driven
Structure depends on mass and composition
330) Compact object scales
White dwarf support from electron degeneracy
Neutron star support from neutron degeneracy and nuclear forces
Schwarzschild radius: Rs = 2GM/c²
Surface gravity can be enormous for compact objects
Escape speed near black holes approaches c
Observables include redshift and accretion signatures
331) Gravitational waves
Strain h describes fractional length change
Wave amplitude is extremely small for distant astrophysical sources
Quadrupole radiation dominates in general relativity
Frequency and chirp rate reveal source masses
Interferometers measure tiny path differences
Signal extraction uses matched filtering
332) Magnetohydrodynamic balance
J × B acts as magnetic force density
Pressure gradient can balance magnetic pressure
Flux freezing in ideal MHD moves field with fluid
Alfvén speed: vA = B/√(μ0ρ)
Plasma beta compares gas pressure to magnetic pressure
MHD applies in astrophysical and laboratory plasmas
333) Transport coefficients
Diffusivity D governs mass transport
Thermal conductivity k governs heat transport
Viscosity η governs momentum transport
Mobility μ relates drift velocity to field
Conductivity depends on carrier density and mobility
Coefficients are often temperature dependent
334) Continuum conservation laws
Mass conservation: ∂ρ/∂t + ∇·(ρv) = 0
Momentum conservation: ρDv/Dt = ∇·σ + body forces
Energy conservation tracks internal, kinetic, and potential forms
Flux terms represent transport across boundaries
Integral and differential forms are equivalent under smoothness conditions
335) Wave equation solutions
Traveling wave: f(x - vt) or f(x + vt)
General 1D solution is sum of left- and right-moving waves
Standing wave arises from superposition of opposite traveling waves
Boundary conditions quantize allowed wavelengths
Wave speed depends on medium properties
Initial conditions select amplitudes and phases
336) Heat diffusion equation
∂T/∂t = α∇²T
α = k/(ρc)
Higher diffusivity spreads temperature faster
Steady state satisfies ∇²T = 0 with no sources
Boundary values and geometry control temperature profile
Analogous to diffusion of concentration and charge in some limits
337) Electrostatic energy relations
U = 1/2 ∫ρV dV for charge distributions
Energy density: u = 1/2 εE²
Point-charge pair energy: U = k q1q2/r
Work required to assemble charges equals stored field energy
Potential energy decreases when opposite charges approach
Field energy formulation often simplifies geometry
338) Magnetic energy relations
Energy density: u = B²/(2μ0) in vacuum
Inductor energy: U = 1/2 LI²
Mutual inductance stores coupled energy
Magnetic fields store and transfer energy in circuits and space
Changing magnetic energy induces emf
Energy accounting is central in electromechanical systems
339) Circuit transient combinations
RC and RL transients use exponentials with time constants
Multiple time constants can arise in complex networks
Step response is found from differential equations or Laplace methods
Initial and final values constrain solutions
Transient analysis predicts charging, switching, and decay behavior
340) Summary extension
Advanced physics often reduces to a small set of ideas: conservation laws, symmetry, boundary conditions, and linear response
Most formulas above are special cases of those principles
Use dimensions, limiting cases, and units to check results
341) Dimensional constants and scaling
Scaling law form: y ∝ x^n
If x doubles, y changes by factor 2^n
Characteristic scale analysis often reveals dominant terms
Dimensionless groups reduce parameter count
Similarity requires matching key dimensionless numbers
342) Estimation and order of magnitude
Fermi estimate = simplified reasoning from known scales
Keep only leading factors and powers of ten
Check whether result is physically plausible
Estimate first, refine later
Useful for sanity-checking calculations
343) Error bars and confidence
Absolute uncertainty: Δx
Relative uncertainty: Δx/x
For independent sums, variances add
For products, relative variances combine
Confidence interval width shrinks with larger sample size
344) Measurement resolution
Resolution = smallest distinguishable change
Sensitivity = output change per input change
Accuracy = closeness to true value
Precision = repeatability
Calibration corrects systematic offsets
345) Signal and noise
SNR = signal/noise
Noise power adds from independent sources
Averaging reduces random noise roughly as 1/√N
High SNR improves detectability
Bandwidth affects total noise captured
346) Logarithmic scales
Decibel power ratio: 10 log10(P2/P1)
Decibel amplitude ratio: 20 log10(A2/A1)
Magnitude scales are logarithmic
Large dynamic ranges are compressed conveniently
Common in acoustics, optics, and electronics
347) Harmonic analysis
Any periodic function can be decomposed into harmonics
Fundamental frequency sets the base period
Higher harmonics modify waveform shape
Fourier coefficients encode harmonic content
Orthogonality of sine and cosine basis functions is key
348) Vectors in mechanics
Force, velocity, acceleration, momentum, and torque are vector quantities
Scalar quantities include mass, energy, time, and temperature
Resolve vectors into components before combining
Magnitude alone is not enough for direction-dependent problems
Unit vectors describe direction only
349) Component method
Vx = V cos θ
Vy = V sin θ
Resultant magnitude: V = √(Vx² + Vy²)
Direction: θ = tan^-1(Vy/Vx)
Use signs consistently in each quadrant
350) Relative velocity reminders
vA/B = vA - vB
vB/A = -vA/B
For river problems, velocity relative to water and water relative to ground both matter
For wind problems, use vector addition
Train, boat, and rain questions are all relative motion problems
351) Angular geometry
Arc length: s = rθ
Sector area: A = 1/2 r²θ
Radians are dimensionless but must be treated carefully
Small-angle formulas require θ in radians
Angular displacement is a signed quantity
352) Kinematic graphs
Slope of x-t graph = velocity
Slope of v-t graph = acceleration
Area under v-t graph = displacement
Area under a-t graph = change in velocity
Curvature of x-t graph reflects acceleration
353) Force-time graph
Area under F-t graph = impulse
Impulse = change in momentum
Large force over short time can equal small force over long time
Impact problems often use impulse directly
Average force equals area divided by duration
354) Work from graphs
Area under F-x graph = work
Positive area adds energy to system
Negative area removes energy from system
Only force component parallel to displacement does work
Friction usually does negative work
355) Spring systems
Hooke’s law: F = -kx
Potential energy: U = 1/2 kx²
Oscillation frequency: ω = √(k/m)
Stiffer spring means larger k and higher frequency
Extension is proportional to force in linear range
356) Energy bookkeeping
Initial energy + input = final energy + losses
Potential energy can convert to kinetic energy and vice versa
Nonconservative forces remove mechanical energy
Total energy is conserved in isolated systems
Choose a convenient zero level for potential energy
357) Momentum bookkeeping
Total momentum before = total momentum after in isolated system
Internal forces cancel pairwise
External impulse changes system momentum
Momentum conservation applies in vector form
Center-of-mass motion summarizes system translation
358) Center of mass
r_cm = Σmi ri / Σmi
v_cm = Σmi vi / Σmi
a_cm = Σmi ai / Σmi
For uniform objects, CM lies at geometric center
System motion splits into CM motion and internal motion
359) Collision types
Elastic collision conserves momentum and kinetic energy
Inelastic collision conserves momentum only
Perfectly inelastic objects stick together
Coefficient of restitution measures rebound
Momentum conservation is the first tool to apply
360) Circular motion reminders
v = ωr
a_c = v²/r = ω²r
F_c = mv²/r
Centripetal force is the net inward force, not a new type of force
Direction of velocity is tangent to path
361) Vertical motion
y = y0 + v0y t - 1/2 gt²
vy = v0y - gt
At top, vy = 0
Free-fall acceleration is constant near Earth
Use sign convention consistently
362) Projectile decomposition
x-motion and y-motion are independent
Horizontal acceleration is zero if air resistance is ignored
Vertical acceleration is -g or +g by sign convention
Time of flight comes from vertical motion
Range comes from combining both components
363) Rotational variables
θ = θ0 + ω0 t + 1/2 αt²
ω = ω0 + αt
α = dω/dt
Torque causes angular acceleration
Angular quantities mirror linear kinematics
364) Rotational energy
Krot = 1/2 Iω²
Total kinetic energy for rolling: 1/2 mv² + 1/2 Iω²
Rotation about larger moment of inertia stores more energy at same ω
Parallel-axis theorem shifts the axis contribution
Mass distribution strongly affects I
365) Rolling motion
Rolling without slipping: v = ωR
a = αR
Static friction provides the needed torque in many rolling problems
Rolling combines translation and rotation
Energy conservation often simplifies rolling motion
366) Torque
τ = rF sin θ
τ = Fd⊥
Torque is maximal when force is perpendicular to lever arm
Opposite torques can cancel in equilibrium
Direction follows right-hand rule
367) Equilibrium conditions
ΣF = 0
Στ = 0
Static equilibrium requires both translational and rotational balance
Choose torque axis to eliminate unwanted forces
Free-body diagrams are essential
368) Friction again
Static friction adjusts up to a maximum value
fk = μk N for kinetic friction model
Friction opposes relative motion or impending motion
Friction can do negative work
Friction coefficient depends on surfaces
369) Inclined plane
Parallel component of weight: mg sin θ
Perpendicular component: mg cos θ
Normal force often equals mg cos θ if no other vertical forces act
Down-slope acceleration without friction: g sin θ
With friction: g(sin θ - μk cos θ) when sliding downhill
370) Gravitation summary
F = Gm1m2/r²
g = GM/r²
U = -GMm/r
v_orbit = √(GM/r)
v_esc = √(2GM/r)
Inverse-square law governs central attraction
371) Orbital speed and period
v = √(GM/r) for circular orbit
T = 2π√(r³/GM)
Kepler’s third law: T² ∝ r³
Orbital speed decreases with larger radius
Escape speed exceeds orbital speed by √2 factor
372) Keplerian motion
Orbits are ellipses with central body at one focus
Equal areas are swept in equal times
Closer approach means higher speed
Energy and angular momentum determine orbit shape
Eccentricity describes orbit elongation
373) Fluid density and pressure
ρ = m/V
P = F/A
Hydrostatic pressure: P = P0 + ρgh
Deeper fluid means higher pressure
Pressure acts equally in all directions at a point
374) Buoyancy
FB = ρfluid g Vdisplaced
Buoyant force equals weight of displaced fluid
Object floats when buoyancy balances weight
Sink if weight exceeds maximum buoyant force
Archimedes’ principle follows from pressure difference
375) Continuity
A1v1 = A2v2 for incompressible steady flow
Narrower tube means faster flow
Flow rate Q = Av
Mass flow rate = ρAv
Continuity reflects mass conservation
376) Bernoulli relation
P + 1/2 ρv² + ρgh = constant along streamline in ideal flow
Higher speed corresponds to lower pressure in same height idealized flow
Used in pipes, lift, and jets
Assumes nonviscous, incompressible, steady flow
Energy per unit volume is conserved along a streamline
377) Surface tension
F = γL
Surface tension acts along the interface
Smaller droplets require greater internal pressure
Surface energy depends on area
Important in droplets, bubbles, and capillary rise
378) Capillary rise
h = 2γ cos θ / (ρgr)
Smaller capillary radius produces larger rise
Contact angle affects wetting
Rise height depends on density and surface tension
Important in narrow tubes and porous media
379) Heat and temperature
Temperature measures average microscopic kinetic energy in idealized models
Heat is energy transfer due to temperature difference
Q = mcΔT
Q = mL for phase change
Thermal equilibrium means equal temperature
380) Expansion
ΔL = αL0ΔT
ΔA = βA0ΔT
ΔV = γV0ΔT
For isotropic solids, β ≈ 2α and γ ≈ 3α
Thermal stress can arise when expansion is constrained
381) Calorimetry
ΣQ = 0 in isolated mixing problems
Heat lost by one body equals heat gained by another
Use consistent sign convention
Latent heat matters during melting, boiling, and condensation
Final temperature comes from energy balance
382) Conduction
Q/t = kAΔT/L
Higher conductivity transfers heat faster
Longer path reduces heat flow
Larger area increases heat flow
Thermal resistance analogy is useful
383) Radiation heat transfer
P = εσA(T⁴ - Tenv⁴)
Stefan-Boltzmann law for blackbody radiation is P = σAT⁴ when ε = 1 and environment is neglected
Emission rises strongly with temperature
Surface emissivity affects radiative loss
Radiation can dominate at high temperature
384) Ideal gas law
PV = nRT
PV = NkBT
R = 8.314 J/(mol·K)
Absolute temperature must be in kelvin
Microscopic and macroscopic forms are equivalent
385) Gas processes
Isothermal: PV = constant
Isobaric: V/T = constant
Isochoric: P/T = constant
Adiabatic: PV^γ = constant
Work by gas = ∫P dV
386) First law of thermodynamics
ΔU = Q - W
Q positive when heat enters system
W positive when system does work on surroundings in common convention
Internal energy depends on state, not path
Work and heat depend on process
387) Heat engines
η = Wout/Qin
η = 1 - Qout/Qin
Efficiency cannot exceed Carnot efficiency
Real engines always have losses
Useful output is work per cycle
388) Carnot cycle
ηCarnot = 1 - Tc/Th
Depends only on reservoir temperatures
Sets ideal upper bound on engine efficiency
Reversible cycle is the limiting case
Used to compare all heat engines
389) Refrigerators and heat pumps
COPref = Qc/W
COPhp = Qh/W
Moving heat from cold to hot requires work input
Performance improves when temperature lift is smaller
Refrigeration and heat pumping are inverse engine concepts
390) Entropy
ΔS = Qrev/T
For isolated system, ΔS ≥ 0
Entropy is a state function
Reversible processes produce no entropy
Irreversible processes generate entropy
391) SHM
x = A cos(ωt + φ) or A sin(ωt + φ)
v = -Aω sin(ωt + φ)
a = -ω²x
ω = 2πf = 2π/T
Maximum speed: vmax = Aω
Maximum acceleration: amax = Aω²
392) Mass-spring oscillator
ω = √(k/m)
T = 2π√(m/k)
E = 1/2 kA² = 1/2 mω²A²
Stiffer spring means shorter period
Energy oscillates between kinetic and potential forms
393) Simple pendulum
For small angles: T = 2π√(L/g)
ω = √(g/L)
Small-angle approximation: sin θ ≈ θ
Period depends on length, not mass, in the ideal model
Valid when angle is small
394) Damped oscillations
x = Ae^(-βt) cos(ωdt + φ)
ωd = √(ω0² - β²)
Amplitude decays exponentially
Critical damping is the boundary between oscillatory and non-oscillatory return
Overdamped motion returns slowly without oscillation
395) Driven oscillations
Steady-state amplitude depends on driving frequency and damping
Resonance occurs near natural frequency
Phase lag changes across resonance
Forced systems can absorb large energy near resonance
RLC circuits are electrical analogues
396) Waves
v = fλ
ω = 2πf
k = 2π/λ
y = A sin(kx - ωt + φ)
Wave speed depends on medium properties
Transverse and longitudinal waves are both possible
397) Superposition
Resultant displacement equals sum of displacements
Wave interference follows superposition principle
Constructive interference increases amplitude
Destructive interference reduces amplitude
Linear systems obey superposition
398) Beats
fbeat = |f1 - f2|
Beats arise from close frequencies
Amplitude envelope varies slowly compared with carrier
Useful in tuning instruments
Beat frequency equals difference between source frequencies
399) Standing waves
String fixed at both ends: λn = 2L/n
fn = nv/(2L)
Closed pipe: λn = 4L/n for odd n only
fn = nv/(4L), n odd
Nodes and antinodes are stationary
400) Sound
v = fλ
Intensity I = P/A
Sound level β = 10 log10(I/I0)
I0 = 10^-12 W/m²
Sound is a mechanical wave that needs a medium
401) Doppler effect
f' = f(v ± vo)/(v ∓ vs)
Observed frequency changes with motion of source and observer
Approach raises frequency, recession lowers it in standard cases
Used in radar and astronomy
Sign choice depends on relative motion direction
402) Reflection
Angle of incidence = angle of reflection
Mirror law applies to smooth reflecting surfaces
Image orientation depends on mirror geometry
Incident and reflected rays lie in same plane
Specular reflection preserves angular relation
403) Mirrors
1/f = 1/do + 1/di
m = hi/ho = -di/do
Concave mirror typically has positive focal length by common convention
Convex mirror typically has negative focal length by common convention
Real and virtual images differ by image distance sign
404) Refraction
n = c/v
n1 sin θ1 = n2 sin θ2
Light bends toward the normal in higher-index medium
Light bends away from the normal in lower-index medium
Index describes optical speed reduction
405) Total internal reflection
sin θc = n2/n1 for n1 > n2
Occurs only when light goes from higher to lower refractive index
Incident angle greater than critical angle gives reflection only
Used in fiber optics and prisms
Requires no transmitted refracted ray beyond the interface
406) Lenses
1/f = 1/do + 1/di
m = hi/ho = -di/do
Converging lens: f > 0
Diverging lens: f < 0
Image nature depends on object position relative to focal point
407) Lens maker relation
1/f = (n - 1)(1/R1 - 1/R2)
Curvature and refractive index determine focal length
More curved surfaces generally give shorter focal length
Sign convention must be used consistently
Applies to thin lenses in simple models
408) Optical power
P = 1/f
Units are diopters when f is in meters
Converging lens has positive power
Diverging lens has negative power
Lens power adds approximately for thin lenses in contact
409) Wave optics
d sin θ = mλ for double-slit maxima
d sin θ = (m + 1/2)λ for double-slit minima
