a sin θ = mλ for single-slit minima
Diffraction and interference arise from wave nature of light
Smaller apertures spread waves more
410) Polarization
I = I0 cos² θ
Malus’s law for ideal polarizers
Unpolarized light through one ideal polarizer gives half intensity
Polarization selects vibration direction
Useful in optics, LCDs, and stress analysis
411) Charge
q = ne
e = 1.602 × 10^-19 C
Charge is quantized in integral multiples of elementary charge
Like charges repel, unlike charges attract
Conservation of charge holds in isolated systems
412) Coulomb’s law
F = k q1q2/r²
k = 1/(4πε0)
Force acts along line joining charges
Inverse-square dependence is central
Vector form required for direction
413) Electric field
E = F/q
E = kq/r² for point charge magnitude
Field direction is away from positive charge and toward negative charge
Fields superpose vectorially
Field lines never cross
414) Electric potential
V = U/q
V = kq/r for a point charge
E = -∇V in general
Potential is scalar
Potential differences drive charge motion
415) Capacitors
C = Q/ΔV
C = εA/d for parallel plates
U = 1/2 CV²
U = 1/2 QV = Q²/(2C)
Capacitance measures charge storage per voltage
416) Capacitor combinations
Parallel: Ceq = ΣC
Series: 1/Ceq = Σ(1/C)
Parallel capacitors share the same voltage
Series capacitors share the same charge
Equivalent capacitance simplifies networks
417) Dielectrics
ε = κε0
Dielectric constant κ increases capacitance in ideal cases
Polarization reduces effective internal field
Bound charges appear in material response
Important in insulating materials between plates
418) Current
I = dQ/dt
Current is flow of charge per time
Conventional current points from positive to negative
Electron flow is opposite conventional current
Steady current means constant charge flow rate
419) Resistance
V = IR
R = ρL/A
Resistance depends on material, length, and cross section
Longer wire gives larger resistance
Thinner wire gives larger resistance
420) Power in circuits
P = IV = I²R = V²/R
Electrical power is rate of energy transfer
Resistive heating converts electrical energy to thermal energy
Choose the form that matches known variables
Power is measured in watts
421) Series and parallel resistors
Series: Req = R1 + R2 + ...
Parallel: 1/Req = 1/R1 + 1/R2 + ...
Series keeps same current through each resistor
Parallel keeps same voltage across each branch
Equivalent resistance simplifies circuit analysis
422) Kirchhoff rules
Junction rule: ΣIin = ΣIout
Loop rule: ΣΔV = 0 around any closed loop
Use current directions consistently
Voltage gains and drops must sum to zero in a loop
Kirchhoff laws follow conservation of charge and energy
423) Battery with internal resistance
Vterminal = ε - Ir
Terminal voltage falls when current increases
Internal resistance causes energy loss inside battery
Open-circuit voltage equals emf when I = 0
Load behavior depends on source resistance
424) RC circuits
τ = RC
Charging: q = Q(1 - e^-t/RC)
Charging voltage: Vc = V(1 - e^-t/RC)
Discharging: q = Qe^-t/RC
Exponential behavior is set by time constant
425) RL circuits
τ = L/R
Current growth: I = (V/R)(1 - e^-Rt/L)
Inductor opposes changes in current
Energy stored in magnetic field
Transient response decays exponentially
426) Magnetism
F = qvB sin θ
Force is perpendicular to velocity and magnetic field
Magnetic force does no work on a point charge in ideal case
Direction found by right-hand rule
Motion is often circular or helical
427) Force on wire
F = ILB sin θ
Current-carrying wire experiences magnetic force
Direction from current and field orientation
Used in motors and actuators
Maximal when wire is perpendicular to field
428) Circular motion in B-field
qvB = mv²/r
r = mv/(qB)
ω = qB/m
T = 2πm/(qB)
Charged particles can move in circles under magnetic force
429) Magnetic field from wire
B = μ0I/(2πr)
Field circles the wire
Strength decreases with distance
Right-hand thumb rule gives direction
Common symmetry result
430) Magnetic field from loop and solenoid
B = μ0I/(2R) at center of loop
B ≈ μ0nI for ideal solenoid
Solenoid field is nearly uniform inside
More turns increase field strength
Useful in electromagnets
431) Magnetic flux
ΦB = ∫B·dA
For uniform field, ΦB = BA cos θ
Flux measures field passing through a surface
Angle matters through cosine factor
Basis for electromagnetic induction
432) Faraday’s law
ε = -dΦB/dt
N-turn coil: ε = -N dΦB/dt
Changing flux induces emf
Negative sign expresses Lenz’s law
Induction requires flux change
433) Lenz’s law
Induced current opposes change in flux
Direction is determined by flux change, not by strength alone
Prevents violation of energy conservation
Important for generators and braking systems
Opposition is to change, not to flux itself
434) Transformers
Vp/Vs = Np/Ns
Ip/Is = Ns/Np for ideal transformer
Power approximately conserved: VpIp = VsIs
Works with changing magnetic flux
Requires AC or changing current
435) Inductors
εL = -L dI/dt
U = 1/2 LI²
Inductor resists changes in current
Stores energy in magnetic field
Time constant in RL circuits is L/R
436) AC circuits
V(t) = Vmax sin(ωt + φ)
Irms = Imax/√2
Vrms = Vmax/√2
XC = 1/(ωC)
XL = ωL
437) Impedance
Z = √(R² + (XL - XC)²)
Imax = Vmax/Z
Impedance generalizes resistance for AC
Phase angle depends on reactive components
Resonance occurs when XL = XC in series RLC
438) Average AC power
Pavg = Vrms Irms cos φ
Power factor = cos φ
Resistive load has φ = 0
Purely reactive load has zero average power
Useful for energy transfer calculations
439) Resonance
Resonance occurs when driving frequency matches natural frequency
In series RLC, resonance often when XL = XC
Then impedance is minimum and current is maximum
Energy oscillates between electric and magnetic forms
Peak response depends on damping
440) Electromagnetic waves
c = λf
c = 1/√(μ0ε0)
E = cB for plane waves
Electric and magnetic fields are perpendicular to each other and to propagation
EM waves do not need a medium
441) Relativity
γ = 1/√(1 - v²/c²)
Δt = γΔt0
L = L0/γ
p = γmv
E = γmc²
442) Relativistic energy
E0 = mc²
K = (γ - 1)mc²
E² = (pc)² + (mc²)²
Photons have m = 0 and E = pc
Classical formulas fail near light speed
443) Photoelectric effect
hf = φ + Kmax
Kmax = hf - φ
eVs = Kmax
Threshold frequency f0 = φ/h
Light intensity controls count rate, not maximum electron energy
444) De Broglie wavelength
λ = h/p
Particle-wave duality links momentum and wavelength
Shorter wavelength corresponds to larger momentum
Basis of electron diffraction
Important in quantum mechanics
445) Uncertainty principle
Δx Δp ≥ ħ/2
ΔE Δt ≥ ħ/2
Sharper position implies larger momentum spread
Not a measurement flaw, but a fundamental limit
Quantum states have intrinsic spread
446) Bohr hydrogen model
En = -13.6 eV/n²
ΔE = hf = hc/λ
L = nħ
Electron transitions produce spectral lines
Hydrogen spectrum follows discrete levels
447) Rydberg formula
1/λ = R(1/n1² - 1/n2²)
n2 > n1 for emission
Describes hydrogen spectral lines
Series correspond to final quantum number n1
Useful for atomic spectroscopy
448) Radioactive decay
N = N0 e^-λt
A = λN
t1/2 = ln 2 / λ
Mean life τ = 1/λ
Decay is exponential in time
449) Binding energy
EB = [ZmH + (A - Z)mn - mnucleus]c²
Mass defect converts to binding energy
More binding per nucleon usually means greater stability
Nuclear masses determine released energy
Energy follows mass-energy equivalence
450) Mass-energy equivalence
E = mc²
Small mass corresponds to large energy
Used in nuclear reactions and particle physics
Rest energy is present even at zero momentum
Energy and mass are equivalent in relativistic physics
451) Useful constants
g ≈ 9.8 m/s²
c ≈ 3.00 × 10^8 m/s
G ≈ 6.67 × 10^-11 N·m²/kg²
k ≈ 8.99 × 10^9 N·m²/C²
R = 8.314 J/(mol·K)
NA = 6.022 × 10^23 mol^-1
e = 1.602 × 10^-19 C
h = 6.626 × 10^-34 J·s
ħ = 1.055 × 10^-34 J·s
452) Small-angle approximations
sin θ ≈ θ
tan θ ≈ θ
cos θ ≈ 1 - θ²/2
Valid when θ is in radians and small
Useful in pendulum, optics, and wave problems
453) Binomial approximation
(1 + x)^n ≈ 1 + nx for |x| << 1
Higher-order terms can be neglected when x is small
Common in error propagation and weak perturbations
Approximation simplifies nonlinear expressions
Use only when justified by magnitude of x
454) Graph slope and area
Physics often reads directly from graphs
Slope gives rate of change
Area gives accumulated quantity
Intercept gives initial value or offset
Graph interpretation is a major problem-solving tool
455) Conservation checklist
Mass conservation in fluid flow
Charge conservation in circuits and electromagnetism
Momentum conservation in isolated systems
Energy conservation in isolated systems
Angular momentum conservation when net external torque is zero
456) Symmetry insight
Symmetry simplifies calculation
If symmetry forbids a direction, the corresponding vector component is zero
Uniform spherical symmetry suggests radial fields
Cylindrical symmetry suggests dependence on radius only
Use symmetry before heavy algebra
457) Units conversion
1 N = 1 kg·m/s²
1 J = 1 N·m
1 W = 1 J/s
1 Pa = 1 N/m²
1 C = 1 A·s
1 V = 1 J/C
458) Dimensional checks
Every equation must be dimensionally homogeneous
Dimensionless quantities may be arguments of trig, exponential, and logarithm functions
Different terms being added must share the same dimensions
Dimensional errors reveal algebra mistakes quickly
Always check before finalizing an answer
459) Final mechanics summary
ΣF = ma
Wnet = ΔK
p = mv
J = Δp
τ = Iα
L = Iω
These relations connect force, motion, energy, and rotation
460) Final fields summary
F = qE
F = qvB sin θ
V = IR
C = Q/ΔV
ε = -dΦB/dt
Gauss and Maxwell laws unify electrostatics and magnetism
461) Final waves summary
v = fλ
ω = 2πf
k = 2π/λ
Interference, diffraction, and standing waves all follow superposition
Boundary conditions create allowed modes
462) Final modern physics summary
E = hf
λ = h/p
ΔxΔp ≥ ħ/2
E = mc²
N = N0e^-λt
Modern physics replaces classical intuition at small scales and high speeds
463) Tensor basics
Tensor rank indicates number of indices
Kronecker delta: δij = 1 if i=j, else 0
Levi-Civita symbol εijk changes sign under index swaps
Contraction reduces tensor rank
Tensor transformation law preserves physical form
464) Einstein summation convention
Repeated indices imply summation
AiBi = Σi AiBi
AijBj = Σj AijBj
Compact notation simplifies tensor equations
Free indices must match on both sides
465) Stress tensor
Force per area in arbitrary direction is represented by σij
Traction on a surface: ti = σij nj
Symmetric stress tensor in ordinary continua under angular momentum balance
Principal stresses are eigenvalues of the stress tensor
Shear and normal components depend on orientation
466) Strain tensor
Small-strain tensor: εij = 1/2(∂ui/∂xj + ∂uj/∂xi)
Displacement field: u(x)
Volumetric strain = trace(ε)
Shear strain appears in off-diagonal components
Linear elasticity assumes small deformation
467) Hooke’s law in tensor form
σij = Cijkl εkl
Cijkl is the elasticity tensor
Isotropic media reduce to two elastic constants
Stress is linearly related to strain in the elastic regime
Symmetry reduces the number of independent coefficients
468) Continuum energy density
Kinetic energy density = 1/2 ρv²
Elastic energy density = 1/2 σij εij
Field energy density can be integrated over volume
Energy balance includes flux through boundaries
Energy density is useful in wave and material problems
469) Euler equations for inviscid flow
ρ(Dv/Dt) = -∇p + ρg
No viscosity term appears in the inviscid limit
Often used for ideal fluids
Combines pressure gradient and body force acceleration
Reduces to Bernoulli-like relations in steady flow
470) Navier–Stokes in component form
ρ(∂vi/∂t + vj∂vi/∂xj) = -∂p/∂xi + μ∂²vi/∂xj² + ρgi
Nonlinear advection term causes complexity
Viscous diffusion smooths velocity gradients
Incompressibility adds ∂vi/∂xi = 0
Boundary conditions are essential
471) Stream function idea
For 2D incompressible flow, u = ∂ψ/∂y and v = -∂ψ/∂x
Streamlines are contours of ψ
Automatically satisfies continuity
Useful in planar fluid problems
Vorticity relates to Laplacian of ψ in simple settings
472) Vorticity
ω = ∇ × v
Measures local rotation of a fluid element
Zero vorticity indicates irrotational flow
Helicity can be formed from v · ω
Vorticity dynamics are central in turbulence
473) Turbulence scaling
Reynolds number Re = ρvL/μ
Large Re often favors turbulent flow
Kolmogorov scale marks smallest eddies in idealized turbulence
Energy cascades from large to small scales
Exact turbulence theory remains complex
474) Boundary layer thickness
For flat plate laminar flow, δ grows with distance downstream
Boundary layers form where viscous effects matter near surfaces
Skin friction depends on boundary layer development
Separation can occur when flow reverses near a surface
Important in drag and lift
475) Lift and circulation
Lift per unit span: L' = ρvΓ
Γ is circulation around body
Pressure differences around a wing create lift
Idealized models use potential flow plus circulation
Angle of attack affects lift generation
476) Potential flow
v = ∇φ for irrotational flow
Laplace equation: ∇²φ = 0 in incompressible potential flow
Can be superposed from source, sink, and dipole flows
Useful idealization for smooth flows
Neglects viscosity and vorticity generation
477) Source and sink flow
Radial velocity in 2D source flow varies as 1/r
Flux conservation determines velocity field
Potential has logarithmic dependence in 2D
Source-sink pairs model idealized flow patterns
Can be combined with uniform flow
478) Conformal mapping idea
Angle-preserving transformation in 2D
Useful for solving some potential problems
Maps complex geometries to simpler ones
Complex potential encodes velocity and stream function
Common in ideal fluid and electrostatic analogies
479) Complex potential
W(z) = φ + iψ
dW/dz gives complex velocity in 2D potential flow
Streamlines and equipotentials are orthogonal
Analogy with electrostatics is strong
Helps solve canonical flow problems
480) Electrostatic potential energy of distributions
U = 1/2 ∫ρV dV
Also U = ε0/2 ∫E² dV in vacuum
Energy can be written as field energy or charge-potential energy
Useful for continuous charge distributions
Factor 1/2 avoids double counting
481) Capacitance from geometry
Capacitance depends on conductor shape and dielectric medium
Sphere: C = 4π ε0 R in vacuum
Coaxial cable and cylindrical geometries have logarithmic dependence
Parallel-plate formula is idealized
Fringing fields alter real capacitance
482) Conductors in electrostatic equilibrium
Electric field inside conductor is zero
Excess charge resides on surface
Surface is an equipotential
Field just outside surface is normal to conductor
Charge density is higher at sharper curvature
483) Method of images
Used to solve electrostatic boundary value problems
Replace conductors by fictitious charges
Potential satisfies boundary conditions exactly in ideal setups
Common for point charge near grounded plane or sphere
Reduces difficult geometry to simpler superposition
484) Multipole expansion
V(r) ≈ monopole + dipole + quadrupole + ...
Far-field potential often dominated by lowest nonzero multipole
Dipole term falls faster with distance than monopole
Quadrupole and higher terms describe finer structure
Useful in atomic, molecular, and gravitational fields
485) Electric dipole moment
p = qd
Torque on dipole: τ = p × E
Potential energy: U = -p · E
Field of dipole falls approximately as 1/r³ far away
Important in molecules and antennas
486) Magnetic dipole moment
μ = IA n̂ for a current loop
Torque: τ = μ × B
Potential energy: U = -μ · B
Magnetic moment governs alignment in fields
Basis for many magnetostatics and spin phenomena
487) Hall voltage and carrier density
VH = IB/(nqt)
Hall coefficient RH = 1/(nq) in simple single-carrier model
Sign of RH indicates dominant carrier sign
Useful for measuring charge carrier density
Widely used in condensed matter experiments
488) Cyclotron motion
Angular frequency ωc = qB/m
Cyclotron frequency is independent of speed in nonrelativistic limit
Radius r = mv⊥/(qB)
Used in particle accelerators and plasma confinement
Energy increases with radius at fixed B
489) Betatron condition
Electron acceleration in changing magnetic flux
Average magnetic field relation maintains circular orbit during acceleration
Induced electric field does work on charge
Conceptual basis for some accelerator systems
Links induction to particle dynamics
490) Synchrotron radiation scaling
Radiation increases strongly with particle energy and curvature
Power is higher for lighter particles at same energy
Important limitation in circular accelerators
Relativistic beaming narrows emission angle
Essential in modern high-energy storage rings
491) Quantum angular momentum algebra
[Li, Lj] = iħ εijk Lk
Raising and lowering operators change m by ±1
L± = Lx ± iLy
Useful in solving angular eigenvalue problems
Operator structure underlies spin and orbital coupling
492) Spin-1/2 states
Sz eigenvalues are ±ħ/2
Pauli matrices represent spin operators
Spinors transform differently from ordinary vectors
Measurement along an axis yields discrete outcomes
Central to magnetic resonance and quantum information
493) Pauli matrices
σx, σy, σz are 2×2 Hermitian matrices
σiσj = δijI + i εijk σk
Spin operators can be written as S = (ħ/2)σ
Matrix algebra simplifies two-level systems
Used in quantum mechanics and optics
494) Two-level system dynamics
Rabi oscillation frequency depends on driving strength and detuning
Population can oscillate between two states
Bloch sphere represents state geometry
Coherent driving and relaxation compete
Useful in lasers, qubits, and spectroscopy
495) Bloch sphere relations
Any pure qubit state corresponds to a point on a sphere
North and south poles often represent basis states
Rotation on sphere corresponds to unitary evolution
Phase is encoded in azimuthal angle
Visual tool for two-state quantum systems
496) Schrödinger equation
iħ ∂ψ/∂t = Ĥψ
Time evolution is unitary for closed systems
Hamiltonian operator determines dynamics
Stationary states satisfy Ĥψ = Eψ
Foundation of nonrelativistic quantum mechanics
497) Probability current
j = (ħ/2mi)(ψ∇ψ - ψ∇ψ)
Continuity equation: ∂ρ/∂t + ∇·j = 0
ρ = |ψ|² = ψ*ψ
Ensures probability conservation
Useful in tunneling and scattering
498) WKB approximation
Semiclassical wavefunction used when potential varies slowly
Action integral controls phase accumulation
Classically allowed and forbidden regions are treated differently
Tunneling probability estimated from exponential integral
Bridge between classical and quantum mechanics
499) Perturbation theory
E = E0 + E(1) + E(2) + ...
First-order correction often equals expectation of perturbing Hamiltonian in unperturbed state
Higher orders account for coupling between states
Useful when exact solution is difficult
Common in atomic and molecular physics
500) Degenerate perturbation theory
Degenerate states require diagonalization within degenerate subspace
Perturbation matrix determines splitting
Physical eigenstates are linear combinations of original basis states
Important in symmetry breaking and spectroscopy
Removes ambiguity in equal-energy levels
Rows: 500
Table 2
Physics Formula Bank
501) Fermi golden rule
Γ = (2π/ħ)|⟨f|H'|i⟩|² ρ(Ef)
Matrix element and final density of states control rates
Used in emission, absorption, and scattering
Time-dependent perturbations produce transitions
Widely applied in quantum optics and condensed matter
502) Density of states
Number of states per energy interval
Influences heat capacity, conductivity, and optical response
3D free-electron DOS grows as √E
Lower-dimensional systems have different functional forms
Critical in semiconductor and quantum gas physics
503) Fermi energy
At zero temperature, states fill up to EF
Fermi-Dirac distribution determines occupancy at finite T
Fermi temperature TF = EF/kB
Electrons in metals are often degenerate
Explains many low-temperature properties
504) Sommerfeld model
Free-electron gas with quantum statistics
Electronic heat capacity proportional to T at low temperature
Provides better metallic behavior than classical model
Uses quantum state filling and Fermi surface
Foundation of simple solid-state theory
505) Band structure relations
Periodic potential leads to allowed bands and forbidden gaps
Bloch states: ψk(r) = uk(r)e^(ik·r)
Effective mass comes from curvature of E(k)
Electrons near band edges behave as if mass is altered
Explains conductors, semiconductors, and insulators
506) Effective mass
1/m* = (1/ħ²)(d²E/dk²)
Curvature of band structure determines response to fields
Negative curvature can produce hole-like behavior
Important for transport in crystals
Used in semiconductor device modeling
507) Phonon occupancy
n̄ = 1/(e^(ħω/kBT) - 1)
Bose-Einstein statistics for lattice vibrations
Thermal population increases with temperature
Phonons mediate heat transport in insulators
Quantum behavior matters at low temperature
508) Debye model
Approximates phonon spectrum with cutoff frequency
Explains low-temperature heat capacity trend
Debye temperature θD characterizes lattice stiffness
Heat capacity approaches classical limit at high T
Major result in solid-state physics
509) Einstein model of solids
Atoms treated as independent quantum oscillators
Predicts temperature-dependent heat capacity
Simpler than Debye model but less accurate at low T
Shows quantized vibrational energy levels
Useful historical and conceptual model
510) Semiconductor intrinsic relation
np = ni²
Electron and hole concentrations linked in equilibrium
Doping shifts carrier balance
Temperature changes intrinsic concentration strongly
Core relation in device physics
511) Shockley diode equation
I = Is(e^(qV/nkBT) - 1)
Forward bias produces exponential current rise
Reverse current saturates near -Is in ideal model
n is ideality factor
Central approximation for p-n junctions
512) MOS capacitor idea
Gate voltage controls surface potential
Oxide acts as dielectric barrier
Accumulation, depletion, and inversion regimes occur
Underlying principle of MOSFET operation
Charges are controlled electrostatically
513) MOSFET current regions
Linear region current depends on gate overdrive and drain voltage
Saturation occurs when channel pinches off near drain
Threshold voltage separates conduction regimes
Widely used transistor model
Approximate equations depend on device geometry
514) Blackbody and photon number
Photon occupation follows Bose-Einstein statistics with zero chemical potential
Energy density depends on temperature fourth power
Shorter wavelengths dominate at higher temperature
Planck spectrum resolves ultraviolet catastrophe
Foundation of quantum radiation theory
515) Compton shift
Δλ = h/(mec)(1 - cos θ)
Photon scatters from electron with wavelength increase
Angle determines magnitude of shift
Demonstrates particle nature of light
Important in high-energy scattering
516) Rutherford scattering
Differential cross section scales roughly as 1/sin⁴(θ/2)
Describes Coulomb scattering from pointlike nucleus
Large-angle scattering is rare
Historical evidence for nuclear atom structure
Useful in particle and nuclear experiments
517) Cross section and mean free path
Mean free path λmfp = 1/(nσ)
n is number density and σ is collision cross section
Higher density or larger cross section shortens mean free path
Relevant in gases, radiation transport, and plasmas
Determines collision frequency
518) Transport in gases
Diffusion coefficient often scales with mean free path and thermal speed
Viscosity and thermal conductivity arise from molecular motion
Knudsen number compares mean free path to system size
Rarefied gas behavior differs from continuum flow
Important in vacuum and high-altitude physics
519) Partition function
Z = Σ exp(-βEi)
β = 1/(kBT)
Thermodynamic quantities follow from Z
Free energy F = -kBT ln Z
Central object in statistical mechanics
520) Thermodynamic averages
⟨E⟩ = -∂ ln Z/∂β
Heat capacity from energy fluctuations
Entropy and pressure can be derived from free energy
Statistical ensemble bridges micro and macro descriptions
Powerful tool in equilibrium physics
521) Linear response theory
Output response x(t) = ∫χ(t - t')F(t')dt'
Frequency domain: x(ω) = χ(ω)F(ω)
Static susceptibility χ(0) measures weak-field response
Complex χ contains dispersion and dissipation
Common in dielectrics, magnetism, and transport
522) Green’s functions
L G(x, x') = δ(x - x') for linear operator L
Solution can be written as u(x) = ∫G(x, x')f(x')dx'
Boundary conditions are embedded in G
Retarded Green function enforces causality
Useful for fields, waves, and diffusion
523) Noether theorem
Continuous symmetry implies a conserved quantity
Time translation symmetry gives energy conservation
Spatial translation symmetry gives momentum conservation
Rotation symmetry gives angular momentum conservation
Gauge symmetry underlies charge conservation
524) Poisson brackets
{f, g} = Σi(∂f/∂qi ∂g/∂pi - ∂f/∂pi ∂g/∂qi)
Time evolution: df/dt = {f, H} + ∂f/∂t
{qi, pj} = δij
Canonical variables preserve bracket structure
Classical analogue of quantum commutators
525) Hamilton–Jacobi equation
H(q, ∂S/∂q, t) + ∂S/∂t = 0
Action function S generates canonical transformation
Separable solutions can simplify integrable systems
Links classical mechanics to wave-like phase evolution
Often useful in central-force and optics problems
526) Action-angle variables
Ji = (1/2π)∮pi dqi
Angles evolve linearly in time for integrable systems
Hamiltonian becomes H = H(J)
Frequencies: ωi = ∂H/∂Ji
Powerful for periodic bound motion
527) Symplectic structure
Phase-space element is preserved under canonical flow
Symplectic form ω = Σ dqi ∧ dpi
Liouville theorem follows from phase-volume conservation
Canonical maps preserve Hamiltonian form
Foundation of geometric mechanics
528) Field Lagrangian density
Action S = ∫L d^4x for fields
Euler–Lagrange field equation: ∂L/∂φ - ∂μ(∂L/∂(∂μφ)) = 0
Canonical momentum density π = ∂L/∂(∂0φ)
Hamiltonian density follows from Legendre transform
Used in classical and quantum field theory
529) Four-vectors
Xμ = (ct, x, y, z)
Pμ = (E/c, px, py, pz)
Invariant scalar product uses Minkowski metric
Proper time τ is frame invariant along worldline
Contractions of four-vectors give observer-independent quantities
530) Lorentz transformation
t' = γ(t - vx/c²)
x' = γ(x - vt)
y' = y, z' = z
γ = 1/sqrt(1 - v²/c²)
Preserves spacetime interval for inertial frames
531) Electromagnetic field tensor
Fμν combines electric and magnetic fields into one object
Field equations take compact covariant form
Electric and magnetic fields mix under boosts
Tensor antisymmetry encodes field structure
Useful in relativistic electrodynamics
532) Energy-momentum tensor
Tμν describes flow of energy and momentum
Local conservation: ∂μTμν = 0 in closed systems
Stress and energy density are components of Tμν
Source term in Einstein field equations
Central in continuum and relativistic field theory
533) Geodesic motion
d²xμ/dτ² + Γμ αβ (dxα/dτ)(dxβ/dτ) = 0
Γμ αβ are Christoffel symbols
Particles follow extremal proper-time paths in gravity
Reduces to straight-line motion in flat spacetime
Basis of orbital motion in general relativity
534) Weak-field gravity
Metric component g00 ≈ -(1 + 2Φ/c²)
Newtonian potential Φ appears as small perturbation
Acceleration approximately g = -∇Φ
Gravitational time dilation follows from metric potential
Connects relativity to classical gravity limit
535) Gravitational redshift
fobs / fem = sqrt((1 + 2Φem/c²)/(1 + 2Φobs/c²)) in weak field
Higher gravitational potential lowers observed frequency
Equivalent wavelength increase for climbing photons
Important near dense stars and in precision timing
Direct probe of spacetime curvature
536) Gravitational lensing
Deflection angle for point mass scales like 4GM/(bc²)
Einstein radius depends on lens and source distances
Multiple images and arcs can form from strong lensing
Magnification changes with source alignment
Used in cosmology and dark-matter mapping
537) FLRW line element
ds² = -c²dt² + a(t)²[dχ² + S_k(χ)² dΩ²]
Scale factor a(t) captures cosmic expansion
Curvature index k distinguishes open, flat, closed models
Homogeneity and isotropy are built into the metric
Foundation of standard cosmology
538) Distance measures in cosmology
Proper distance = a(t) comoving distance
Luminosity distance dL = (1 + z)² dA if reciprocity holds
Angular diameter distance relates size to observed angle
Comoving distance integrates c/H(z)
Different observables use different distance definitions
539) Expansion dynamics
H(t) = ȧ/a
Deceleration parameter q = -a ä/ȧ²
For accelerating expansion, q < 0
Critical density ρc = 3H²/(8πG)
Energy content determines expansion history
540) Horizon scales
Particle horizon limits causal contact
Hubble radius often approximated by c/H
Comoving horizon grows differently from physical horizon
Inflation stretches causal regions
Important for CMB and structure formation
541) Temperature scaling of radiation
Photon temperature scales roughly as Tγ ∝ 1/a in adiabatic expansion
Number density of radiation drops with expansion
Energy density of radiation scales as a^-4
One factor from dilution, one from redshift
Key in early-universe thermodynamics
542) Debye screening
Potential in plasma or electrolyte decays as exp(-r/λD)/r
λD = sqrt(εkBT/(ne²)) in simple form
Electrostatic fields are screened over finite range
Higher temperature or lower density increases λD
Important in plasmas and colloids
543) Plasma frequency
ωp = sqrt(ne²/(mε0)) for electron plasma oscillations
Below ωp, waves may not propagate in ideal plasma
Collective oscillations dominate response
Determines reflection and cutoff behavior
Central in ionized media physics
544) Landau quantization
Electron energy in magnetic field forms discrete Landau levels
Level spacing proportional to ħωc
ωc = eB/m for nonrelativistic motion
Degeneracy increases with field strength and area
Basis of quantum Hall physics
545) Zeeman splitting
ΔE = μB g mB B for magnetic sublevels in simple form
External magnetic field lifts degeneracy of angular momentum states
Normal and anomalous Zeeman effects differ by spin involvement
Spectral line splitting measures magnetic field strength
Used in spectroscopy and magnetometry
546) Stark effect
External electric field shifts and splits atomic levels
Linear Stark effect can appear in degenerate states
Quadratic Stark shift often scales as E²
Polarizability determines level sensitivity
Important in atomic and molecular spectroscopy
547) Spin-orbit coupling
Interaction energy proportional to L · S
Fine structure arises from coupling of orbital and spin angular momentum
Splits atomic terms with same n and l
Important correction in heavy atoms
Explains multiplet structure in spectra
548) Fine-structure correction
Includes relativistic correction, spin-orbit coupling, and Darwin term
Energy shifts are small compared with principal level spacing
Corrections scale strongly with atomic number
Explains detailed hydrogen spectral splitting
Requires quantum electrodynamic refinement at higher precision
549) Hyperfine structure
Interaction between nuclear spin and electronic angular momentum splits levels further
Energy depends on total angular momentum F
Important for atomic clocks and spectroscopy
Hyperfine transition frequency is highly stable in selected atoms
Magnetic dipole coupling is the dominant term
550) Franck–Condon principle
Electronic transitions occur faster than nuclear motion
Transition intensity depends on vibrational wavefunction overlap
Vertical transitions dominate in absorption and emission
Explains vibrational structure in molecular spectra
Useful in photochemistry and spectroscopy
551) Raman scattering
Scattered photon shifts by vibrational or rotational energy difference
Stokes shift lowers photon frequency
Anti-Stokes shift raises photon frequency
Raman activity depends on polarizability change
Non-destructive probe of molecular structure
552) Brillouin scattering
Light scatters from acoustic phonons or density waves
Frequency shift is much smaller than ordinary Raman shifts
Probes sound velocity and elastic properties
Occurs in fluids, solids, and optical fibers
Useful in material characterization
553) Stopping power
-dE/dx describes energy loss per unit path length
Approximate Bethe-type dependence increases with charge squared and electron density
Slow charged particles lose energy strongly near end of range
Range depends on material and particle type
Used in radiation shielding and detector design
554) Multiple scattering
Many small-angle deflections accumulate along path
Angular spread grows with thickness and material density
RMS angle often scales with sqrt(x/X0)
Important in beam transport and radiation physics
Limits spatial resolution in detectors
555) Shock waves
Conservation across shock gives Rankine–Hugoniot relations
Pressure, density, and velocity change abruptly
Occurs when flow becomes supersonic relative to disturbance propagation
Shock compression heats gas
Important in aerodynamics and astrophysics
556) Mach cone geometry
sin μ = c/v for supersonic source motion
Mach angle μ decreases as speed increases
Characteristic of sonic booms and shocks
Applied to high-speed aircraft and projectiles
Flow disturbances cannot outrun the source
557) Compressible flow relations
Density is not constant when Mach number is significant
Isentropic approximations link T, P, and ρ changes
Nozzle area changes can accelerate or decelerate flow
Choked flow occurs at sonic throat condition
Relevant in rockets, turbines, and jets
558) Boundary-layer thickness scaling
Laminar boundary thickness grows roughly as delta ~ sqrt(nu x / U)
Shear stress is concentrated near solid surfaces
Thicker layer forms downstream
Flow separation reduces lift and increases drag
Viscosity matters even when bulk flow is nearly inviscid
559) Vortex shedding
Alternating vortices are released behind bluff bodies
Strouhal number St = fL/U
Shedding frequency depends on speed and geometry
Can induce oscillations and structural vibration
Important in bridges, chimneys, and cylinders
560) Fracture mechanics
Stress intensity K characterizes crack-tip loading
Crack growth can occur when K exceeds critical toughness
Energy release rate G measures available fracture energy
Long cracks amplify local stress
Used in material failure prediction
561) Paris law for fatigue
da/dN = C(Delta K)^m
Crack growth rate depends on cyclic stress intensity range
C and m are material constants in empirical fits
Fatigue life is determined by integrated crack growth
Relevant for aircraft, bridges, and rotating machinery
562) Dislocation motion
Burgers vector b characterizes lattice defect
Slip occurs when resolved shear stress exceeds critical value
Plastic deformation in crystals often proceeds by dislocation glide
Dislocation density affects hardness and yield strength
Key mechanism in metallurgy and solid mechanics
563) Lattice thermal conductivity
kappa ~ (1/3) C v l in kinetic picture
C is heat capacity per volume, v sound speed, l mean free path
Phonon scattering reduces heat transport
Impurities and boundaries shorten l
Explains temperature dependence of insulating solids
564) Diffusion length
Ld = sqrt(D tau)
Carriers or particles travel typical distance before recombination or decay
Longer lifetime or larger diffusivity increases diffusion length
Useful in semiconductors, biology, and transport
Sets device collection efficiency
565) Thermoelectricity
Seebeck voltage V = S Delta T
Peltier heat rate Qdot = Pi I
Thomson heat involves current in temperature gradient
Figure of merit ZT measures device performance
Couples heat and charge transport
566) Peltier and Seebeck reciprocity
Pi = S T in ideal thermoelectric relation
Thermoelectric devices convert temperature differences to voltage and vice versa
Efficiency improves with large Seebeck coefficient and low thermal conductivity
Used in cooling and power harvesting
Material optimization is a major design challenge
567) Piezoelectricity
Mechanical strain generates electric polarization in non-centrosymmetric crystals
Direct effect: stress -> charge
Inverse effect: electric field -> strain
Constitutive coupling links stress, strain, and field
Used in sensors, actuators, and ultrasonics
568) Ferroelectric hysteresis
Polarization P switches with applied electric field
Hysteresis loop shows remanent polarization and coercive field
Domain structure determines response
Phase transition often occurs at Curie temperature
Used in memory and capacitor materials
569) Dielectric loss
Loss tangent tan delta = epsilon''/epsilon'
Represents dissipative response in alternating field
Higher loss means more heating and lower efficiency
Important in materials at RF and microwave frequencies
Complex permittivity captures storage and dissipation
570) Josephson relations
I = Ic sin phi
dphi/dt = 2eV/hbar
Supercurrent flows without voltage below critical current
AC Josephson effect links voltage and frequency
Key ingredient in superconducting circuits
571) SQUID basics
Interference of superconducting phases controls critical current
Flux periodicity is one flux quantum
Extremely sensitive magnetic flux sensor
Output depends on applied flux through loop
Used in precision magnetometry and quantum devices
572) NMR relaxation
T1 describes longitudinal relaxation
T2 describes transverse dephasing
Signal decays with characteristic relaxation times
Spin echo can recover dephasing from inhomogeneity
Used in spectroscopy and medical imaging
573) ESR resonance
Electron spins resonate in magnetic field at microwave frequencies
Resonance condition uses g factor and magnetic field strength
Probes paramagnetic centers and radicals
Sensitive to local electronic environment
Analogue of NMR for electron spins
574) Semiconductor continuity equations
Charge conservation for electrons: dn/dt = generation - recombination + transport terms
Similar equation holds for holes
Current continuity couples carrier density and motion
Basis of device modeling
Important in diodes, transistors, and solar cells
575) Drift-diffusion current
Jn = q n mu_n E + q D_n grad n
Jp = q p mu_p E - q D_p grad p
First term is drift under electric field
Second term is diffusion from concentration gradient
Einstein relation links diffusion and mobility in equilibrium settings
576) Depletion region width
W depends on doping and built-in potential in p-n junctions
Higher reverse bias widens depletion layer
Lower doping gives wider depletion
Electric field peaks near the junction
Critical to diode capacitance and breakdown
577) Minority-carrier diffusion
Excess minority concentration decays exponentially with distance
Characteristic decay length is diffusion length
Recombination lifetime limits collection
Key in photovoltaic and diode response
Transport beyond depletion region matters
578) Recombination kinetics
Rate often modeled as R = (n - n0)/tau in simple linear approximation
Radiative, Auger, and trap-assisted recombination are distinct channels
Larger carrier density can increase recombination strongly
Lifetime controls transient response
Important in semiconductors and scintillators
579) Small-signal transistor gain
gm = dIc/dVbe in BJT small-signal model
Incremental gain depends on bias point
Output resistance and transconductance set amplifier behavior
Linearization around operating point simplifies circuit analysis
Used in analog electronics
580) MOSFET threshold behavior
Channel inversion occurs when gate voltage exceeds threshold
Drain current depends on gate overdrive in different regions
Subthreshold current rises exponentially with gate bias
Body effect shifts threshold
Central to digital and analog integrated circuits
581) Ideal op-amp rules
Input currents approximately zero
With negative feedback, input voltages nearly equal
Inverting gain = -Rf/Rin
Non-inverting gain = 1 + Rf/Rg
Idealization simplifies amplifier networks
582) Feedback gain
Closed-loop gain = open-loop gain / (1 + loop gain)
Negative feedback stabilizes response and reduces sensitivity
Positive feedback can create oscillation or hysteresis
Bandwidth often increases with feedback
Core concept in control and electronics
583) Transfer functions
H(s) = output(s)/input(s)
Poles determine natural response
Zeros shape frequency response
Impulse response is inverse Laplace of H(s)
Used to characterize linear systems
584) Filter roll-off
Low-pass attenuation increases beyond cutoff frequency
High-pass attenuation dominates below cutoff
Band-pass has a finite pass window
Order of filter determines roll-off slope
Important in instrumentation and signal conditioning
585) Waveguide cutoff
Propagation occurs only above cutoff frequency for a given mode
Below cutoff, fields decay exponentially along guide
Geometry sets modal cutoff
TE and TM modes have distinct boundaries
Used in microwave and optical structures
586) Cavity finesse and Q
Finesse measures sharpness of resonance peaks
Q = stored energy divided by energy lost per cycle times 2pi
High-Q cavities store energy longer
Linewidth narrows as Q increases
Important in lasers, spectroscopy, and resonators
587) Interferometer path difference
Interference depends on optical path difference, not just geometric distance
Constructive interference occurs when path difference equals integer wavelengths
Destructive interference occurs at half-integer wavelengths
Michelson and Fabry-Perot geometries use this principle
Precision metrology tool
588) Thin-film interference
Reflections from upper and lower interfaces interfere
Phase reversal on reflection depends on refractive index contrast
Condition for color bands depends on film thickness and wavelength
Path difference often includes 2nt cos theta
Explains soap bubbles and coatings
589) Diffraction limit
Minimum resolvable angle scales like 1.22 lambda/D for circular aperture
Smaller aperture gives poorer resolution but larger depth of field
Finite aperture creates Airy pattern
Fundamental wave limitation in imaging systems
Relevant in microscopes and telescopes
590) Rayleigh criterion
Two point sources are barely resolved when central maximum of one overlaps first minimum of the other
Angular resolution limited by aperture and wavelength
Different instruments have different criteria
Provides practical imaging benchmark
Used in optics and astronomy
591) Fourier optics
Far-field diffraction pattern is Fourier transform of aperture function in ideal paraxial limit
Spatial frequencies determine image detail
Lens performs Fourier-like mapping at focal plane
Phase and amplitude both matter
Basis of modern imaging and holography
592) Partial-wave scattering
Scattering amplitude decomposes into angular momentum channels
Phase shifts delta_l encode interaction strength
Cross section is sum over partial waves
Low-energy scattering often dominated by few terms
Common in quantum and nuclear physics
593) Born approximation
Weak potential gives scattering amplitude proportional to Fourier transform of potential
First-order perturbative scattering valid for weak interactions
Useful for X-rays, electrons, and neutrons in simple regimes
Breaks down for strong potentials
Bridge between potential and observed angular pattern
594) Optical theorem
Total cross section is proportional to imaginary part of forward scattering amplitude
Connects measurable attenuation with coherent forward amplitude
Encodes unitarity of scattering
Important in particle and wave scattering theory
Nontrivial consistency check for models
595) S-matrix idea
S transforms incoming asymptotic states to outgoing states
Unitarity preserves total probability
Matrix elements encode transition amplitudes
Central object in scattering theory
Useful across quantum field and particle physics
596) Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution
f(v) = 4pi (m/2pi kT)^(3/2) v^2 exp(-mv^2/2kT)
Most probable, mean, and rms speeds differ by numerical factors
Classical limit valid when quantum degeneracy is negligible
Describes dilute gases well
Foundation of kinetic theory
597) Fermi-Dirac occupation
f(E) = 1/(exp((E - mu)/kT) + 1)
Pauli exclusion limits occupancy to one per state per spin in idealization
At low T, states fill up to Fermi level
Determines electron transport in metals and semiconductors
Quantum statistics for fermions
598) Bose-Einstein occupation
f(E) = 1/(exp((E - mu)/kT) - 1)
Valid for bosons with no exclusion constraint
Low-temperature pileup in low-energy states can occur
Underlying statistics of photons, phonons, and some atoms
Leads to condensation phenomena
599) Bose-Einstein condensation
Condensation occurs when thermal wavelength becomes comparable to interparticle spacing
Critical temperature depends on density and particle mass
Macroscopic occupation of ground state emerges
Quantum coherence becomes visible on large scales
Important in ultracold atom physics
600) Fermi gas pressure
Degeneracy pressure arises even at zero temperature
Pressure scales with density as a quantum effect
Supports white dwarfs and neutron stars in different regimes
Not due to thermal motion alone
Consequence of Pauli exclusion
601) London equations
dJs/dt = (ns e^2/m) E
curl Js = -(ns e^2/m) B
Explain zero resistance and Meissner effect in phenomenological form
Penetration depth sets field decay inside superconductor
Foundation of superconducting electrodynamics
602) Flux quantization
Magnetic flux through superconducting loop is quantized in units of h/(2e)
Phase coherence enforces discrete flux states
Observed in superconducting rings and qubits
Direct evidence for paired charge carriers
Central to quantum superconducting circuits
603) Superfluid circulation
Circulation is quantized in integer multiples of h/m in idealized superfluids
Vortices have discrete winding number
Flow can persist without classical viscosity
Macroscopic wavefunction controls hydrodynamics
Characteristic of helium and ultracold atomic systems
604) Brownian motion
Mean squared displacement grows linearly with time: ~ 2Dt in 1D
Random kicks from thermal environment drive diffusion
Diffusion constant depends on temperature and friction
Stochastic motion underlies many transport processes
Bridge between microscopic and macroscopic behavior
605) Langevin equation
m dv/dt = -gamma v + random force
Damping and noise compete in thermal motion
Leads to diffusion and fluctuation relations
Useful in Brownian particles and noisy circuits
Stochastic differential equation model
606) Fluctuation-dissipation relation
Noise strength is linked to dissipative coefficient in thermal equilibrium
Response and fluctuations are not independent
Explains thermal noise in resistors and Brownian systems
General principle across statistical physics
Connects linear response to equilibrium variance
607) Johnson-Nyquist noise
Voltage noise spectral density: Sv = 4kTR
Current noise depends on conductance
Thermal noise exists even without applied signal
Sets fundamental limit in electronics
Important in low-noise measurement design
608) Radiative transfer equation
dI/ds = -kappa I + j
Absorption reduces intensity, emission adds to it
Source function S = j/kappa in simple form
Used in atmosphere, stellar interiors, and plasma diagnostics
Path-integral solution depends on optical depth
609) Planck spectrum in frequency form
u(nu, T) = (8pi h nu^3 / c^3) / (exp(hnu/kT) - 1)
Describes blackbody energy density per frequency interval
Limits at low and high frequency recover classical and exponential regimes
Solves ultraviolet catastrophe
Foundational quantum result
610) Rayleigh-Jeans limit
At low frequency, blackbody spectrum approaches classical proportionality to nu^2 T
Valid when hnu is small compared with kT
Classical approximation fails at high frequency
Illustrates necessity of quantization
Useful limiting form for long wavelengths
611) Bateman decay chain
Sequential radioactive decays obey coupled linear differential equations
Parent and daughter populations evolve with distinct decay constants
Solutions are sums of exponentials
Useful for nuclear chain analysis and age dating
Generalizes single exponential decay
612) Reaction rate equations
dn/dt = production - loss
Linearized kinetics often take form dn/dt = a n + b
Steady state satisfies production equals loss
Common in nuclear, chemical, and plasma kinetics
Time constants govern approach to equilibrium
613) Neutron moderation
Average logarithmic energy decrement measures slowing effectiveness
Light nuclei slow neutrons more efficiently per collision
Elastic scattering dominates moderation in many reactors
Thermalization changes reaction cross sections
Important in reactor physics
614) Resonant cross sections
Cross section can peak sharply near resonance energy
Breit-Wigner form models many resonances
Width relates to lifetime via uncertainty principle
Used in nuclear and particle scattering
Explains strong energy dependence of interactions
615) Detector dead time
Observed count rate differs from true rate due to processing time
Nonparalyzable model: nobs = ntrue / (1 + ntrue tau)
Paralyzable model behaves differently at high rates
Critical in radiation counting systems
Correction needed for accurate measurements
616) Counting statistics
Poisson uncertainty sigma = sqrt(N)
Relative uncertainty decreases as 1/sqrt(N)
Independent counts add in mean and variance
Good model for rare-event detection
Used in photonics, nuclear, and particle experiments
617) Maximum likelihood
Choose parameters that maximize probability of observed data
Log-likelihood often easier to optimize
Estimator properties improve with large sample size under regularity conditions
Core method in experimental physics and statistics
Can be combined with Bayesian priors
618) Least-squares normal equations
Minimize sum of squared residuals
Matrix form gives A^T A x = A^T b
Weighted least squares handles unequal variances
Used for parameter estimation and calibration
Closely related to orthogonal projection
619) Covariance propagation
Var(f) ≈ grad f^T Sigma grad f
Covariance matrix Sigma captures correlated errors
Cross terms matter when inputs are not independent
Important in metrology and data analysis
Generalizes simple error propagation
620) Metrology traceability
Measurements are traceable through calibration hierarchy to standards
Uncertainty budget combines random and systematic terms
Bias correction and repeatability are both tracked
Ensures comparability across instruments and labs
Central to high-accuracy experiments
621) Dimensional homogeneity in advanced models
Every additive term must share units
Arguments of exp, log, sin, cos must be dimensionless
Scale analysis can identify dominant physical parameters
Useful for checking derived equations before use
Prevents silent algebraic mistakes
622) Similarity and scaling
If system size changes by factor a, times often scale by a power of a
Reynolds, Mach, and Froude similarity preserve dynamic correspondence
Dimensionless matching enables model testing
Wind tunnels and water tanks rely on this idea
Scaling laws guide experiments and design
623) Similarity in fluid experiments
Geometric similarity alone is not enough
Dynamic similarity needs key dimensionless numbers to match
Boundary conditions and viscosity matter
Model results can fail if dominant nondimensional groups differ
Widely used in aerodynamics and hydraulics
624) Weak measurement scaling
Signal-to-noise often improves like sqrt(N)
Longer integration reduces random error
Bandwidth narrowing reduces measured noise but can slow response
Tradeoff between resolution and speed is universal
Optimization depends on experiment goal
625) Frequency-domain energy
Parseval-type relations connect time and frequency energy content
Broadband signals occupy many spectral components
Filtering redistributes energy across frequency bands
Spectral density is crucial in noise analysis
Widely used in signal processing and physics
626) Convolution systems
Output y = x * h for linear time-invariant systems
Impulse response h fully characterizes the system
Convolution in time corresponds to multiplication in frequency
Used in detectors, optics, circuits, and acoustics
Foundation of system response analysis
627) Causal response
h(t) = 0 for t < 0 in causal systems
Retarded responses respect time ordering
Physical systems cannot respond before excitation
Enforced naturally in real Green functions
Essential in control and field theory
628) Stability criteria
Stable linear systems have poles in the left half-plane in Laplace variable convention
Marginal stability can produce sustained oscillations
Unstable poles grow with time
Root locus tracks pole motion with feedback
Important in circuits, mechanics, and control
629) Resonance width
Linewidth is inversely related to lifetime or damping strength
High Q means narrow resonance
Broad resonance means faster energy leakage
Observed in mechanical, electrical, and atomic systems
Width contains information about dissipation
630) Phase-space portraits
Trajectories in x-v or q-p space show qualitative dynamics
Closed loops indicate periodic motion
Spirals indicate damping or growth
Separatrices divide distinct motion regimes
Helpful for nonlinear and oscillatory systems
631) Nonlinear oscillator frequency shift
Amplitude-dependent frequency can occur in anharmonic potentials
Hardening or softening behavior depends on nonlinearity sign
Small oscillation approximation becomes insufficient at large amplitude
Common in real springs, pendulums, and circuits
Predicts detuning and waveform distortion
632) Duffing oscillator
x¨ + delta x˙ + alpha x + beta x^3 = gamma cos(omega t)
Cubic term introduces nonlinearity
Can show bistability and chaos
Models driven nonlinear vibration
Important in mechanics and MEMS
633) Parametric resonance
Oscillator parameters vary periodically in time
Mathieu-type equations describe the instability regions
Energy can grow by periodic modulation even without direct forcing
Pendulum length modulation is a classic example
Relevant in mechanics and optics
634) Mathieu stability
Solutions alternate between stable and unstable bands
Periodic coefficients create resonance tongues
Used in ion traps and periodically driven systems
Provides a mathematical model of parametric excitation
Highly sensitive to driving frequency
635) Adiabatic invariance
Slow parameter changes conserve certain action-like quantities approximately
Useful for slowly varying oscillators and orbits
Explains some heating and cooling processes in expanded systems
Requires timescale separation
Common in plasma and celestial mechanics
636) Phase velocity and group velocity
vp = omega/k
vg = d omega/dk
Wave packets move with group velocity in simple nondispersive media
Phase and group velocities can differ greatly in dispersive systems
Important in optics and quantum mechanics
637) Dispersion relation
omega = omega(k) encodes medium response
Linear dispersion gives constant wave speed
Nonlinear dispersion causes spreading of pulses
Curvature of omega(k) affects group velocity dispersion
Central to waves in solids, plasmas, and optics
638) Effective medium approximation
Composite response may be approximated by averaged material parameters
Applicability depends on wavelength being larger than microstructure
Used for permittivity, permeability, and conductivity mixtures
Breaks down near resonances or strong contrast
Helpful in metamaterials and composites
639) Metamaterial index
Effective epsilon and mu can be engineered to unusual values
Negative index is possible in certain structured media
Phase and energy flow can point in different directions
Strong dispersion often accompanies exotic response
Used in lenses, cloaking concepts, and antennas
640) Wave impedance
Z = sqrt(mu/epsilon) for a uniform medium in ideal plane-wave form
Controls reflection at boundaries
Matching impedances improves transmission
Different for free space, dielectrics, and magnetic media
Useful in optics and transmission lines
641) Reflection coefficient
Amplitude reflection depends on impedance mismatch
Power reflection is amplitude squared in simple lossless cases
Large mismatch gives strong standing waves
Applied in acoustics, optics, and microwave engineering
Relevant to antireflection design
642) Transmission coefficient
Transmitted amplitude complements reflected amplitude at interfaces
Energy conservation sets relation between reflected and transmitted power in lossless media
Depends on incident angle and polarization in optics
Can be engineered with coatings
Important for sensors and devices
643) Brewster geometry
At Brewster angle reflected light can become fully polarized in ideal cases
Angle depends on refractive indices
Used in polarization optics and laser cavities
Reflection vanishes for one polarization component
Important in optical design
644) Jones calculus
Polarized light can be represented by a two-component complex vector
Optical elements act as matrices on this vector
Phase retarders and polarizers are matrix operators
Useful for coherent polarization analysis
Breaks down for depolarized or partially coherent light
645) Stokes parameters
I, Q, U, V describe polarization state statistically
Useful when light is partially polarized
Can be measured experimentally with analyzers
Complementary to Jones calculus
Important in astrophysical and laboratory polarimetry
646) Mueller matrices
4x4 real matrices transform Stokes vectors
Represent depolarizing and nondepolarizing optical elements
Useful in polarimetry and biomedical optics
Generalizes Jones calculus to partially polarized light
Capable of modeling complex optical systems
647) Kirchhoff radiation law
Good absorbers are good emitters at same wavelength and temperature in thermal equilibrium
Emissivity links absorption and emission behavior
Explains blackbody-like properties of real surfaces
Important in thermal imaging and heat transfer
Depends on spectral and directional conditions
648) Surface emissivity
Real surfaces emit less than ideal blackbodies by factor epsilon
Depends on wavelength, angle, temperature, and material
Polished metal often low emissivity, matte surfaces higher emissivity
Critical in infrared thermometry and heat shielding
Can vary substantially with coating and oxidation
649) Radiative view factor
Fraction of radiation leaving one surface that reaches another depends on geometry
View factors satisfy reciprocity and summation rules
Used in enclosure radiation calculations
Important in thermal design of spacecraft and furnaces
Encodes purely geometric coupling
650) Stefan-Boltzmann-type heat balance
Net radiative exchange can be approximated by emissivity times sigma times area times temperature difference of fourth powers
Relevant when conduction and convection are weak
Strongly nonlinear in temperature
Explains rapid cooling at high temperature
Common in astrophysics and thermal engineering
651) Lorentz force density
f = ρE + J × B
Force per unit volume combines electric and magnetic contributions
Useful in plasmas and continua
Charge density and current density both matter
Links field theory to fluid-like dynamics
652) Maxwell stress tensor
Tij = ε0(EiEj - 1/2 δij E²) + (1/μ0)(BiBj - 1/2 δij B²)
Momentum flux of electromagnetic fields
Surface integral gives net electromagnetic force
Important in radiation pressure and field forces
Compact way to compute forces on objects
653) Radiation pressure
P = I/c for full absorption
P = 2I/c for ideal reflection
Intensity determines momentum transfer rate
Relevant in lasers, solar sails, and astrophysics
Radiation carries momentum as well as energy
654) Poynting theorem
∂u/∂t + ∇·S = -J·E
u = 1/2(ε0E² + B²/μ0)
S = (1/μ0)E × B
Energy conservation for electromagnetic fields
Shows how field energy flows and is dissipated
655) Skin depth
δ = √(2/(μσω))
AC fields penetrate conductors only shallowly
Higher frequency or conductivity reduces penetration depth
Important in shielding and high-frequency circuits
Explains current crowding near surfaces
656) Wave attenuation in lossy media
A(x) = A0 e^(-αx)
Intensity I = I0 e^(-2αx)
Attenuation coefficient α describes decay per distance
Used in optics, acoustics, and electromagnetism
Lossy media absorb wave energy
657) Coherent length and time
τc ≈ 1/Δν
Lc ≈ cτc
Shorter spectral width gives longer coherence
Interference visibility improves with coherence
Important in lasers and precision optics
658) Quality factor of a resonator
Q = 2π(energy stored)/(energy lost per cycle)
Q ≈ ω0/Δω for narrow resonance
Higher Q means slower decay and narrower linewidth
Used in optics, mechanics, and circuits
Measures sharpness of resonance
659) Harmonic oscillator commutator form
[x, p] = iħ
Creation operator a† and annihilation operator a simplify quantum SHM
H = ħω(a†a + 1/2)
Energy level spacing is uniform
Foundation of quantized vibration
660) Creation and annihilation operators
a|n> = √n |n-1>
a†|n> = √(n+1) |n+1>
[a, a†] = 1
Number operator N = a†a
Useful in quantum fields and oscillators
661) Coherent state properties
a|α> = α|α>
Coherent states mimic classical motion best among quantum oscillator states
Minimum uncertainty packets in ideal model
Common in laser theory
Phase space representation is especially useful
662) Wigner function idea
Quasi-probability distribution in phase space
Can take negative values in quantum regimes
Provides bridge between classical and quantum descriptions
Useful in quantum optics and decoherence studies
Represents state information compactly
663) Density matrix
ρ = Σ pi |ψi><ψi|
Pure state satisfies ρ² = ρ
Mixed state represents statistical ensemble
Expectation value: = Tr(ρA)
Useful for open quantum systems
664) Entanglement indicator
Joint state not factorizable into subsystems
Correlations exceed classical separable models
Density matrix of subsystems may be mixed even when total state is pure
Central in quantum information
Measured via entropy or concurrence
665) Decoherence
Environment suppresses off-diagonal density matrix terms
Loss of phase coherence without necessarily losing energy
Explains emergence of classical behavior
Rate depends on coupling to environment
Key limitation in quantum computing
666) Open-system master equation
dρ/dt = -i/ħ [H, ρ] + dissipator
Unitary evolution plus irreversible loss terms
Lindblad form ensures physical density matrix evolution
Used for atoms, cavities, and qubits
Models relaxation and dephasing
667) Bose enhancement
Transition rates for bosons can increase with occupancy
Factor roughly (n + 1) appears in emission processes
Leads to stimulated emission and condensation behavior
Quantum statistical effect unique to bosons
Important in lasers and cold atoms
668) Pauli blocking
Fermions cannot occupy same quantum state
Transition into occupied state is suppressed
Occupancy factor appears as (1 - f)
Crucial in metals, white dwarfs, and nuclear matter
Opposite statistical effect to boson enhancement
669) Fermi surface
Surface in momentum space separating occupied and empty states at T = 0
Dominates transport and low-temperature properties
Shape affected by crystal lattice
Core concept in metallic behavior
Important in condensed matter physics
670) Effective field near surfaces
E⊥ discontinuity depends on surface charge
B⊥ continuous across interface in ordinary media
Boundary conditions determine field transmission
Used for conductors, dielectrics, and magnetic media
Essential for solving interface problems
671) Image charge for grounded plane
Point charge near grounded conductor can be replaced by equal and opposite image charge
Potential satisfies boundary condition on plane
Force follows from interaction with image
Elegant solution method in electrostatics
Shows power of symmetry and superposition
672) Capacitor energy density
u = 1/2 εE²
Energy stored throughout field volume
Not just on conducting plates
Useful in dielectrics and high-voltage design
Connects electrical energy to field intensity
673) Magnetic energy density
u = B²/(2μ0)
Energy stored in magnetic fields
Relevant in inductors, coils, and field regions
Field energy contributes to system dynamics
Analogous to electric energy density
674) Inductor voltage-current relation
V = L di/dt
Changing current produces induced voltage
Opposes current change according to Lenz’s law
Foundation of RL transients
Useful in switching and filtering
675) Mutual inductance
ε2 = -M dI1/dt
Coupling between two circuits through shared flux
Mutual inductance depends on geometry and orientation
Transformer action relies on this principle
Important in coupled circuits
676) Coupled inductor energy
U = 1/2 L1 I1² + 1/2 L2 I2² + M I1 I2
Cross term encodes coupling
Sign depends on winding orientation
Used in transformer and coupled resonator analysis
Describes stored magnetic energy
677) LC oscillator
ω = 1/√(LC)
Energy oscillates between capacitor and inductor
q(t) and I(t) are sinusoidal in ideal model
Electrical analogue of mass-spring system
Ideal oscillation without resistance
678) RLC damping ratio
α = R/(2L) in series RLC convention
ω0 = 1/√(LC)
Underdamped if α < ω0
Critical damping if α = ω0
Overdamped if α > ω0
679) Band-pass resonance width
Δω ≈ R/L for series RLC in narrowband limit
Q ≈ ω0/Δω
Sharp resonance selects frequencies
Used in tuners and filters
Quality factor controls selectivity
680) Transmission line relation
V(z,t) and I(z,t) satisfy wave equations on line
Z0 = √(L'/C') for ideal line
Reflection depends on load mismatch
Propagation speed v = 1/√(L'C')
Important in RF and microwave systems
681) Doppler radar shift
Δf ≈ 2v/λ for reflection from moving target in simple nonrelativistic approximation
Used in speed measurement
Shift depends on radial velocity component
Common in meteorology and traffic radar
Frequency change carries motion information
682) Interferometric fringe shift
ΔN = ΔL/λ
Small path changes create measurable fringe movement
Extremely sensitive displacement measurement
Used in metrology and gravitational-wave detectors
Direct link between phase and distance
683) Michelson interferometer
Path difference between two arms controls output intensity
Constructive and destructive interference alternate with mirror displacement
Fringe count tracks optical path change
Used to measure wavelength and displacement
Foundational precision instrument
684) Fabry–Pérot resonance
2nL cos θ = mλ for transmission maxima in simple condition
Multiple beam interference creates sharp peaks
Free spectral range depends on cavity length
Finesse determines sharpness
Used in spectroscopy and lasers
685) Huygens principle
Each point on a wavefront acts as a source of secondary wavelets
New wavefront is envelope of secondary waves
Explains reflection, refraction, and diffraction qualitatively
Useful conceptual model for wave propagation
Foundational wave optics idea
686) Fresnel number
N_F = a²/(λL)
Distinguishes near-field and far-field diffraction regimes
Large N_F means Fresnel region
Small N_F means Fraunhofer regime
Geometry and wavelength both matter
687) Fraunhofer diffraction
Far-field diffraction pattern is Fourier transform of aperture in ideal limit
Angular distribution depends on aperture shape
Single slit gives sinc-squared intensity pattern
Double slit yields fringes modulated by envelope
Used in optical analysis
688) Gaussian beam waist
Beam radius evolves with distance from waist
Minimum waist w0 determines divergence
Rayleigh range zR = πw0²/λ
Laser beams are often approximated by Gaussian modes
Important in optics and laser engineering
689) Mode volume
Effective mode volume quantifies field confinement
Smaller volume enhances interaction with matter
Important in cavities and photonics
Couples to spontaneous emission and nonlinear effects
Useful in cavity QED
690) Purcell factor
Emission rate enhancement scales with Q/V in cavity context
High Q and small mode volume increase spontaneous emission into cavity mode
Important in quantum optics and photonics
Controls light-matter coupling strength
Useful for emitters in resonators
691) Electromagnetic wave impedance in vacuum
Z0 = √(μ0/ε0)
Relates electric and magnetic amplitudes in free space
Sets power transfer behavior in plane waves
Used in antenna and optics calculations
Fundamental electromagnetic constant relation
692) Relativistic velocity addition
u' = (u + v)/(1 + uv/c²)
Velocities do not add linearly near light speed
Preserves speed limit c
Important in particle kinematics
Follows from Lorentz transformations
693) Proper acceleration
Acceleration measured in instantaneous rest frame
Differs from coordinate acceleration at high speed
Relevant in relativity and accelerated observers
Constant proper acceleration leads to hyperbolic motion
Used in relativistic dynamics
694) Rapidity
η = tanh^-1(v/c)
Rapidities add linearly under boosts in one dimension
Simplifies relativistic velocity composition
Useful in particle physics and relativity
Alternative to velocity parameterization
695) Four-force
Fμ = dpμ/dτ
Relativistic generalization of force
Orthogonal to four-velocity in certain formulations
Useful in covariant dynamics
Combines energy and momentum changes
696) Photon momentum
p = h/λ = E/c
Light carries momentum despite zero rest mass
Accounts for radiation pressure and recoil
Central in optics and atomic physics
Essential in scattering and emission
697) Atomic recoil
Momentum conservation causes atom to recoil on photon emission or absorption
Recoil energy is small but measurable
Important in precision spectroscopy
Limits exact resonance frequencies
Shows momentum exchange between light and matter
698) Doppler cooling
Laser frequency tuned near atomic resonance
Atoms moving toward beam see shifted frequency
Absorption and emission produce net slowing
Requires cooling geometry and detuning choice
Foundation of ultracold atom physics
699) Magnetic trapping
Spatially varying magnetic fields can confine neutral atoms with magnetic moments
Potential energy U = -μ · B in simple form
Trap stability depends on field minimum and spin state
Used in atom cooling and BEC experiments
Combines quantum and classical field effects
700) Optical dipole trap
Intense light field creates effective potential for atoms
Detuning and intensity determine trap depth
Atoms are attracted to intensity maxima or minima depending on detuning sign
Widely used in cold atom experiments
Provides nonmagnetic confinement
701) Thomas-Fermi approximation
Neglects kinetic term in slowly varying density systems
Useful for large trapped Bose gases and electron systems
Gives density profile from balance of trap and interaction
Approximate and context-dependent
Common in ultracold gas theory
702) Gross-Pitaevskii equation
iħ ∂ψ/∂t = [-(ħ²/2m)∇² + V + g|ψ|²]ψ
Mean-field equation for dilute Bose-Einstein condensates
Nonlinear term models interactions
Describes condensate dynamics and vortices
Central in ultracold atom physics
703) Healing length
ξ = ħ/√(2mgn)
Sets length scale over which condensate recovers from disturbances
Smaller interaction or density changes ξ
Important in vortex cores and boundaries
Characteristic of superfluids and condensates
704) Sound in condensates
Speed c_s = √(gn/m)
Low-energy excitations behave as phonons
Collective response differs from single-particle motion
Key to superfluid hydrodynamics
Derived from interaction strength and density
705) Josephson frequency
f = 2eV/h for AC Josephson effect
Voltage-frequency relation is exact in ideal theory
Used in voltage standards
Connects quantum phase evolution to measurable frequency
Superconducting hallmark relation
706) Fluxon dynamics
Quantized flux vortices move in superconductors under current and field
Motion can create dissipation and noise
Vortex pinning improves current-carrying capability
Important in type-II superconductors
Highly relevant in applied superconductivity
707) Critical current density
Jc marks maximum superconducting current before loss of zero-resistance state
Depends on temperature, field, and material defects
Engineering parameter for superconducting wires
Pinning centers can raise Jc
Important in magnets and power devices
708) Type-I and type-II superconductors
Type-I exhibits complete Meissner effect until critical field
Type-II allows mixed state with vortices between lower and upper critical fields
Ginzburg-Landau parameter distinguishes behavior
Material classification impacts applications
Important in superconductivity design
709) Ginzburg-Landau parameter
κ = λ/ξ
Compares penetration depth to coherence length
κ < 1/√2 tends toward type-I behavior
κ > 1/√2 tends toward type-II behavior
Useful classification in superconductors
710) Penetration depth
λ characterizes decay of magnetic field inside superconductor
Field decays exponentially from surface
Depends on carrier density and effective mass in simple models
Important in superconducting electrodynamics
Complementary to coherence length
711) Coherence length
ξ sets scale of order-parameter variation
Related to pair size in superconductivity
Also used in condensate and superfluid contexts
Determines core size of vortices
Key length scale in quantum fluids
712) Debye screening in electrolytes
Electrostatic interactions are screened over finite distance in ionic solutions
Higher ionic strength shortens screening length
Important in soft matter and biophysics
Mathematically similar to plasma screening
Used in colloid and electrolyte theory
713) van der Waals equation
(P + a n²/V²)(V - nb) = nRT
Accounts for molecular attraction and finite size
Improves over ideal gas law near condensation
Predicts phase transition behavior qualitatively
Important thermodynamic correction
714) Critical point relations
At criticality, distinction between liquid and gas vanishes
Response functions can diverge in ideal theory
Correlation length grows large
Fluctuations become scale rich
Important in phase transitions
715) Clapeyron relation
dP/dT = L/(TΔv)
Connects coexistence slope to latent heat and volume change
Useful for phase boundaries
Applies to first-order transitions
Foundation of phase equilibrium analysis
716) Gibbs phase rule
F = C - P + 2
Degrees of freedom depend on components and phases
Useful in phase diagrams
Counts independent intensive variables
Important in materials and chemistry
717) Latent heat
Q = mL
Energy absorbed or released during phase change at constant temperature in idealized model
Different phases have different specific latent heats
Used in melting, boiling, and condensation
Energy goes into changing phase structure
718) Supercooling and nucleation
Phase transition may require nucleation barrier to be overcome
Metastable state can persist below equilibrium transition point
Nucleation rate depends on temperature and barrier height
Explains delayed freezing and condensation
Important in materials and atmospheric physics
719) Surface energy minimization
Systems often evolve to reduce total free energy
Surface tension favors shapes with minimal area
Interfaces and curvature matter
Relevant in droplets, foams, and crystals
Energy minimization guides equilibrium form
720) Young-Laplace pressure
ΔP = γ(1/R1 + 1/R2)
Pressure difference across curved interface
Special cases: soap bubble and sphere geometries
Curvature and surface tension produce pressure jump
Important in droplets and membranes
721) Membrane tension
Force balance on thin membranes uses tension per length
Curvature generates restoring pressure
Important in biology and soft matter
Analogous to surface tension but with thin sheet geometry
Used in bubbles and biological cells
722) Capillary wave
Surface waves on fluids are influenced by gravity and surface tension
Restoring force depends on wavelength regime
Short wavelengths often dominated by surface tension
Long wavelengths often dominated by gravity
Relevant in ocean and interface physics
723) Wave dispersion in fluids
Phase speed depends on wavelength for dispersive waves
Group speed differs from phase speed
Surface waves and deep-water waves disperse
Wave packets spread during propagation
Important in oceanography and hydrodynamics
724) Shallow-water wave speed
v = √(gh)
Wave speed depends on depth, not wavelength in shallow-water limit
Used in tides, tsunamis, and channels
Derived from gravity and fluid depth
Simple but powerful approximation
725) Deep-water wave speed
vph = √(gλ/2π) in ideal gravity-wave limit
Longer waves travel faster in deep water
Group speed differs by factor 1/2 in ideal case
Classical dispersive wave behavior
Important in ocean wave analysis
726) Acoustic resonance tube
Open-open and open-closed geometries give different harmonics
Boundary conditions determine node and antinode positions
Length controls resonant frequencies
Instrument design depends on these modes
Used in pipes, flutes, and organ tubes
727) Helmholtz resonator
Resonant frequency depends on cavity volume and neck geometry
Can be approximated as mass-spring acoustic system
Used in acoustics and noise control
Volume acts like spring, neck air column like mass
Characteristic narrowband resonator
728) Finite-element intuition
Large systems are divided into small elements
Local approximations are assembled into global solution
Useful for elasticity, electromagnetism, and heat flow
Boundary conditions and meshing are crucial
Numerical rather than analytic approach
729) Monte Carlo transport
Random sampling approximates particle paths and interactions
Useful where analytic solutions are hard
Variance decreases with more samples
Widely used in radiation and neutron transport
Probabilistic simulation method
730) Final continuation note
Use this section as a strict continuation of the previous bank without repeating earlier rows
Keep adding later sections in the same single-column format for more breadth
Topic order can continue from mathematical physics into specialized subfields
731) Variational calculus in physics
S[q] = ∫ L(q, q̇, t) dt
δS = 0 gives the physical path
Euler-Lagrange equation: d/dt(∂L/∂q̇) - ∂L/∂q = 0
Generalized coordinates can reduce constraints
Boundary terms vanish for fixed endpoints
732) Lagrangian mechanics reminders
L = T - V
Canonical momentum: p = ∂L/∂q̇
Conjugate momentum differs from ordinary momentum in generalized coordinates
Coordinates may be cyclic if ∂L/∂q = 0
Cyclic coordinate implies conserved momentum
733) Hamiltonian mechanics reminders
H = Σ p_i q̇_i - L
q̇_i = ∂H/∂p_i
ṗ_i = -∂H/∂q_i
Hamiltonian often equals total energy in standard systems
Phase-space flow preserves structure
734) Poisson bracket identities
{f, g} = Σ(∂f/∂q_i ∂g/∂p_i - ∂f/∂p_i ∂g/∂q_i)
Time evolution: df/dt = {f, H} + ∂f/∂t
{q_i, p_j} = δ_ij
{q_i, q_j} = 0 and {p_i, p_j} = 0
Classical analog of commutators
735) Action-angle variables
J = (1/2π)∮ p dq
For periodic motion, angles increase linearly in time
Hamiltonian becomes H(J) for integrable systems
Frequencies: ω = ∂H/∂J
Useful for adiabatic invariance
736) Noether theorem summary
Time symmetry → energy conservation
Spatial symmetry → momentum conservation
Rotational symmetry → angular momentum conservation
Gauge symmetry → charge conservation
Symmetry breaking removes or modifies conserved quantities
737) Classical field equation form
Field Euler-Lagrange: ∂L/∂φ - ∂μ(∂L/∂(∂μφ)) = 0
Action S = ∫ L d^4x
Canonical momentum density: π = ∂L/∂(∂0φ)
Field equations follow from stationary action
Applies to scalar, electromagnetic, and elastic fields
738) Green’s function method
L G(x, x') = δ(x - x')
Solution: u(x) = ∫ G(x, x')f(x')dx'
Boundary conditions are built into G
Retarded Green function enforces causality
Used for waves, diffusion, and Poisson problems
739) Fourier transform toolkit
F(k) = ∫ f(x)e^(-ikx)dx
f(x) = (1/2π)∫ F(k)e^(ikx)dk
Convolution in x becomes multiplication in k
Derivative becomes multiplication by ik
Frequency-domain methods simplify linear systems
740) Laplace transform toolkit
ℒ{f(t)} = F(s)
ℒ{f'} = sF - f(0)
ℒ{f''} = s²F - sf(0) - f'(0)
Heaviside step functions simplify switching problems
